Ash'ari: Human ability without effective power
The author explains the Ash'ari response to the difficulty of al-Ash'ari's denial of effect for human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school, despite denying that created human power has any causal effect, still affirms a genuine human ability and capacity that serves as the basis for religious obligation.

Core Idea
Human ability is real but created—perceived, not self-produced.

This passage tackles a central difficulty in Ash'ari thought: if human power has no effect, how can we speak of human ability and responsibility? The author explains al-Ash'ari's own answer, which avoids making humans mere puppets while preserving divine omnipotence. The key is a distinction between effective power and perceived capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the contrast between 'no effect for power' and the affirmation of 'ability and capacity' that a person perceives.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the problem
The Ash'ari position that created power has no 'athar' (effect) seems to remove human agency. Identify why this is a problem and how the passage addresses it.
3
Grasp the solution: divine custom
Al-Ash'ari explains human perception of ability as stemming from sound constitution and the habitual divine pattern (jaryan al-ada). When a person intends an act, Allah creates power and capacity tied to that act. Notice how this preserves human experience.
4
Connect to obligation (taklif)
The passage states that this perceived ability is 'the locus of obligation'. Reflect on how this allows moral responsibility even though the power itself is not causally effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما على طريقة الشيخ أبي الحسن رحمه الله حيث لم يثبت للقدرة أثراً. فالجواب عن هذه الإلزامات مشكل عليه غير أنه يثبت تأتيا وتمكنا يحسه الإنسان من نفسه، وذلك يرجع إلى سلامة البنية واعتقاد التيسر بحكم جريان العادة أن العبد مهما هم بفعل وأزمع على أمر خلق الله تعالى له قدرة واستطاعة مقرونة بذلك الفعل الذي يحدثه فيه فيتصف به العبد وبخصائصه، وذلك هو مورد التكليف وإحساسه بذلك كإحساسه بالصفات التابعة للحدوث عندكم، وإن لم تكن هي أثر القدرة الحادثة.
As for the method of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan (may Allah have mercy on him) where he did not establish any effect for power, the answer to these objections is difficult for him. However, he establishes ability and capacity that a person perceives from himself. That reverts to soundness of structure and the belief in ease by virtue of the customary course that whenever the servant intends an act and resolves upon a matter, Allah the Most High creates for him power and capacity conjoined with that act which He brings about in him, so the servant is qualified by it and its characteristics. That is the locus of obligation, and his perception of that is like his perception of attributes that are dependent on temporality according to you, even if it is not the effect of the temporal power.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents a key difficulty in the Ash'ari position that created power (al-qudra al-hāditha) has no effect (athar). The author states that al-Ash'ari still affirms a kind of ability (ta'tiya) and capacity (tamakkun) that humans perceive from themselves. This perception is explained as stemming from the soundness of their constitution and the customary divine pattern (jaryān al-‘āda): when a person intends an act, Allah creates power and capacity in him that are tied to that act. Thus, the servant becomes qualified by that act and its characteristics, and this is the basis of obligation (taklīf). The author compares this feeling to the feeling of temporal attributes (like acquired attributes) which are dependent on temporality (hudūth) according to the opponent, even though this is not the effect of the temporal power. This shows the Ash'ari attempt to maintain human responsibility without positing an independent causal power.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Ash'ari denies any human role in actions, making humans passive recipients with no genuine experience of ability.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari affirms a perceived ability and capacity (ta'tiya and tamakkun) that a person experiences from himself, though these are created directly by Allah, not as an effect of the servant's own power.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position described, what does a person perceive from himself when he intends an act?
A) Independent power to create the act
B) Ability and capacity that are created by Allah
C) The effect of his created power on the act
D) A sense of self-sufficiency

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari maintains that human ability is a real, perceived capacity created by Allah according to His custom, providing a basis for obligation without granting causal effect to human power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel capable of performing an act of obedience, do you attribute that feeling to your own strength, or do you recognize it as a direct gift from Allah created at that moment? How would this awareness change how you approach that action?
?
The author says the perception of ability is like the perception of attributes dependent on temporality. Reflect on something you consider 'your' ability—perhaps a skill or a good deed. Can you honestly say that you believe it is created by Allah without any causal effect from your own power? Does your practical reliance on Allah match this belief?
?
If you truly internalized that your intention and resolve are met by Allah creating power conjoined with the act, would you be more or less anxious about your failures? More or less arrogant about your successes? What would change in your daily actions if you lived by this conviction?

Human Ability and Need: The Coexistence of Capacity and Dependence
Empirical analysis of human capacity and need, leading to the correct view on power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari position maintains both human responsibility and complete dependence on Allah, based on empirical observation of human experience.

Core Idea
You sense both ability and need — that is the key to understanding power.

Before discussing the nature of Divine power and human action, the author grounds his argument in lived experience. He points out that every person simultaneously feels capable of acting and utterly dependent on a helper. This simple observation dismantles the Mu'tazilite claim that humans are independent creators of their own actions. Read the passage carefully to see how the author uses everyday examples to build a profound theological point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the detailed examples of thought, speech, and hand movements.
The author uses a very empirical approach here.
2
Identify the two sensations
Find where the author says the servant senses both capacity (tamakkun) and need (iftiqār). Understand that these are not contradictory in the Ash'ari view.
3
Understand the examples
The author gives three examples: reflection, speech, and hand movement. In each case, you can will the action but cannot control all outcomes. Reflect on how this shows dependence.
For instance, you can decide to think but cannot force knowledge to appear.
4
Contrast with the opponent's view
The author describes the Mu'tazilite position: power is equally suited for opposites, and the servant is independent in bringing about actions. See how this differs from your own experience.
5
Grasp the truth affirmed
The author concludes by affirming both capacity and need, denying independence. Note the two attributions: action is attributed to the servant through capacity, but to Allah through creation.
This preserves human accountability while affirming Divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحقيق ذلك من غير جد عن الإنصاف أن العبد كما يحس من نفسه التمكن من الفعل لتيسر التأتي يحس من نفسه الافتقار والاحتياج إلى معين في كل ما يتصرف ويجد في استطاعته، ويتكلف فقدان الاستقلال والاستبداد بالفعل في كل ما يأتي ويذر ويقدم ويؤخر من تصرفات فكره نظراً واستدلالا، ومن حركات لسانه قيلا وقالا، ومن ترددات يديه يمينا وشمالا فيحس الاقتدار على النظر، ولا يحس الاقتدار على العلم بعد حصول النظر، فإنه لو أراد أن لا يحصل لا يتمكن منه ويحس من نفسه تحريك لسانه بالحروف ولو أراد أن يبدل المخارج ويغير الأصوات لم يتمكن من ذلك، ويحس تحريك يديه وأنملته، ولو أراد تحريك جزء واحد من غير تحريك الرباطات المتصلة لم يتمكن من ذلك، وعند الخصم القدرة صالحة للأضداد والأمثال وهي متشابهة في القادرين، فالعبد مستقل بالإيجاد والاختراع، وليس إلى الباري سبحانه وتعالى من هذه الأفعال إلا خلق القدرة فحسب واشتراط البنية من أضعف ما يتصور. والحق في المسألة تسليم التمكن والتأتي والاستطاعة على الفعل على وجه ينتسب إلى العبد وجه من الفعل يليق بصلاحية قدرته واستطاعته، وإثبات الافتقار والاحتياج، ونفي الاستقلال والاستبداد، فنجد في التكليف موردي الخطاب فعلا ويضاف واستطاعة، ويضاف في الجزاء مقابلة وتفضلا والله أعلم.
And the investigation of that without straying from fairness is that the servant, just as he senses from himself the capacity for the act due to the availability of ability, senses from himself need and dependence upon a helper in everything he does, and he finds in his capacity [something?] and takes trouble over the loss of independence and self-sufficiency in every act he does or leaves, advances or postpones, from the movements of his thought, consideration and inference, and from the movements of his tongue, saying and spoken words, and from the movements of his hands right and left. So he senses power over consideration, but he does not sense power over knowledge after the occurrence of consideration. For if he wanted it not to occur, he would not be able to do that. And he senses from himself moving his tongue with letters, but if he wanted to change the articulations and alter the sounds, he would not be able to do that. And he senses moving his hands and fingers, but if he wanted to move one part without moving the connected ligaments, he would not be able to do that. According to the opponent, power is suitable for opposites and likes, and they are similar in those capable. So the servant is independent in bringing about and inventing, and there is nothing from Allah the Most High concerning these acts except the creation of power alone, and stipulating the structure is among the weakest things imaginable. The truth in the matter is to affirm capacity, ability, and power upon the act in a way that a face of the act is attributed to the servant befitting the suitability of his power and capacity, and to affirm need and dependence, and to deny independence and self-sufficiency. So we find in obligation two aspects of the address: an act and it is attributed and capacity, and it is attributed in recompense as a counterpart and favor. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed empirical analysis of human experience. The author argues that a person senses both capacity (tamakkun) and need (iftiqār). He senses the ability to act when the means are facilitated, but simultaneously senses his dependence on a helper in all his actions. He experiences the lack of independence (istiqlāl) and self-sufficiency (istibdād) in every movement—of thought (reflection and inference), of speech (saying and spoken words), and of hands (right and left). For example, he feels able to reflect (naẓar), but cannot guarantee knowledge after reflection; he can move his tongue, but cannot change the articulations at will; he can move his fingers, but cannot move a single component without moving the connected ligaments. According to the opponent (the Mu'tazila), power is equally suited for opposites and likes, so the servant is independent in creation (ījād) and invention (ikhtirā‘). The author rejects this, stating that the truth is to affirm ability and capacity while also affirming need and denying independence. Thus, in obligation (taklīf), the act has two attributions: to the servant in terms of capacity (istiṭā‘a) and to Allah in terms of creation, and in recompense it is attributed as a counterpart (muqābala) and favor (tafaḍḍul).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That affirming human capacity implies human independence from Allah.
Correction: The author shows that capacity and dependence coexist: even though we sense ability, we also sense need for a helper in every action.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does a person sense regarding his actions?
A) Complete independence
B) Only ability
C) Both ability and need for a helper
D) Only powerlessness

Key Takeaway
Human action involves both real capacity and total dependence on Allah; affirming one does not negate the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Recall a specific moment today when you felt both 'I can do this' and 'I need Allah's help to do it.' Did you act on that awareness? If not, what kept you from acknowledging your dependence, and how will you practice that awareness in your next action?
?
The author says you sense power over consideration but not over knowledge. Think of a time you sought understanding—religious or worldly—but could not force it. Did that frustration drive you to supplication or to self-reliance? Starting tomorrow, begin every attempt to learn with a sincere prayer for Allah to grant understanding.
?
The opponents claim that if you have real capacity, you are independent. Do you ever behave as if your successes are solely your own doing? Look at your greatest achievement this month. Can you name, with honesty, the precise ways Allah facilitated it? Now commit to thanking Him for each of those ways today.

The Twin Feelings: Ability and Dependence
Author introduces the dual feeling of ability and need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a person experiences both the ability to act and a sense of need for divine assistance in all actions.

Core Idea
Human action is a mix of ability and dependence, not pure independence.

The passage begins with an empirical observation that anyone can verify: when you act, you feel capable, but you also feel that you rely on something beyond yourself. This sets the foundation for the Ash'ari view, which affirms both the reality of human choice and the ultimate sovereignty of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Read the first sentence carefully. It states that the servant feels both ability (tamakkun) and need (iftiqār) simultaneously.
Note the phrase 'without deviating from fairness' – this is a claim of objectivity.
2
Identify the two feelings
Look for the words al-tamakkun (ability) and al-iftiqār/al-iḥtiyāj (need/dependence). Understand that these are presented as direct human experiences.
3
Consider the scope of these feelings
The passage says this applies to 'everything he performs' – thoughts, speech, and physical actions. This universality is important.
The three domains (thought, speech, action) are listed at the end of this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحقيق ذلك من غير جد عن الإنصاف أن العبد كما يجس من نفسه التمكن من الفعل لوتيسر التأتي يحس من نفسه الافتقار والاحتياج إلى معين في كل ما يتصرف ويجد في استطاعته، ويتكلف فقدان الاستقلال والاستبداد بالفعل في كل ما يأتي ويذر ويقدم ويؤخر من تصرفات فكره نظراً واستدلالا، ومن حركات لسانه قيلا وقالا، ومن ترددات يديه يمينا وشمالا فيحس الاقتدار على النظر، ولا يحس الاقتدار على العلم بعد حصول النظر، فإنه لو أراد أن لا يحصل لا يتمكن منه ويحس من نفسه تحريك لسانه بالحروف ولو أراد أن يبدل المخارج ويغير الأصوات لم يتمكن من ذلك، ويحس تحريك يديه وأنملته، ولو أراد تحريك جزء واحد من غير تحريك الرباطات المتصلة لم يتمكن من ذلك، وعند الخصم القدرة صالحة للأضداد والأمثال وهي متشابهة في القادرين، فالعبد مستقل بالإيجاد والاختراع، وليس إلى الباري سبحانه وتعالى من هذه الأفعال إلا خلق القدرة فحسب واشتراط البنية من أضعف ما يتصور. والحق في المسألة تسليم التمكن والتأتي والاستطاعة على الفعل على وجه ينتسب إلى العبد وجه من الفعل يليق بصلاحية قدرته واستطاعته، وإثبات الافتقار والاحتياج، ونفي الاستقلال والاستبداد، فنجد في التكليف موردي الخطاب فعلا ويضاف في الجزاء مقابلة وتفضلا والله أعلم.
And the verification of that, without deviating from fairness, is that the servant, just as he feels from himself the ability (al-tamakkun) to do the act if the means are facilitated (law tayassara al-ta'tī), he feels from himself need (al-iftiqār) and dependence (al-iḥtiyāj) upon an aider in everything he performs, and he finds in his capacity (istitā‘atihi) and he perceives the lack of independence (al-istiqlāl) and self-sufficiency (al-istibdād) in every act he does and leaves, advances and delays, of the movements of his thought, reflection and inference, and of the movements of his tongue, speaking and saying, and of the movements of his hands, right and left. So he feels power (al-iqtidār) over reflection (al-naẓar), but he does not feel power over knowledge (al-‘ilm) after reflection occurs, for if he wanted it not to occur, he would not be able to do that. And he feels from himself moving his tongue with letters, but if he wanted to change the points of articulation and alter the sounds, he would not be able to do that. And he feels moving his hands and fingers, but if he wanted to move a single part without moving the connected ligaments, he would not be able to do that. And according to the opponent (al-khaṣm), power (al-qudra) is capable of opposites (al-aḍdād) and similars (al-amthāl), and they are similar among those who are powerful (al-qādirīn). So the servant is independent in bringing into existence (al-ījād) and invention (al-ikhtirā‘), and nothing is due to the Creator, exalted, from these acts except the creation of power only, and the condition of the physical constitution (al-bunya) is among the weakest of what can be imagined. And the truth (al-ḥaqq) in the issue is the affirmation of ability (al-tamakkun), facilitation (al-ta'tī), and capacity (al-istiṭā‘a) upon the act in a manner that the servant has a side of the act that befits the fitness of his power and capacity, and the affirmation of need (al-iftiqār) and dependence (al-iḥtiyāj), and the negation of independence (al-istiqlāl) and self-sufficiency (al-istibdād). So we find in obligation (al-taklīf) two sources of address (mūriday al-khiṭāb) as an act, and it is attributed in recompense (al-jazā') as a recompense and a favor. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a refutation-and-correct-statement concerning the nature of human action and divine power. The author begins by establishing empirical evidence: a person feels both ability (tamakkun) to act (if means are facilitated) and a sense of need (iftiqār) and dependence (iḥtiyāj) on a helper in every action. He perceives the lack of independence (istiqlāl) and self-sufficiency (istibdād) in all his acts—whether mental (reflection, inference), verbal (speaking), or physical (hand movements). Then the author introduces a specific contrast: one feels power (iqtidār) over the act of reflection, but not over the knowledge that results after reflection; if one wanted knowledge not to occur, one could not prevent it. Similarly, one can move his tongue to produce letters but cannot arbitrarily change the points of articulation; one can move his hand and fingers but cannot move a single joint without moving connected ligaments. This shows that human power is limited and dependent on God's enabling. The author then presents the opponent (al-khaṣm—the Mu'tazilite) who claims that power (qudra) is capable of opposites (aḍdād) and similars (amthāl) and that all powers are alike in all agents. According to that view, the servant is fully independent in creating and inventing (ījād wa ikhtirā‘), and God's role is merely to create that power and to provide the physical constitution (bunya). The author dismisses this as one of the weakest positions conceivable. Finally, the author states the correct view (al-ḥaqq): we affirm ability (tamakkun), facilitation (ta'tī), and capacity (istiṭā‘a) for the act in a way that the servant has a genuine side (wajh) of the act that befits the fitness of his power and capacity. At the same time, we affirm need (iftiqār) and dependence (iḥtiyāj), and we deny independence (istiqlāl) and self-sufficiency (istibdād). The result is that in obligation (taklīf) we have two sources of address (mūriday al-khiṭāb) for the act, and in recompense (jazā') it is attributed as a recompense and favor. The passage ends with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms
التمكن
الافتقار
الاحتياج
الاستقلال
الاستبداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'aris deny human ability altogether.
Correction: The passage clearly states that the servant feels ability (tamakkun). Ash'aris affirm ability but negate independence (istiqlāl).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the servant feel in addition to the ability to act?
A) He feels complete self-sufficiency.
B) He feels need and dependence upon an aider.
C) He feels no connection to God.
D) He feels only ability, not need.

Key Takeaway
The servant experiences both ability and dependence; human action is never isolated from divine support.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel completely capable of a task—whether thinking, speaking, or moving—do you also sense a hidden thread of need pulling you toward God? What if that thread is not hidden but the very fabric of your action? Will you examine your next thought, word, or movement and see if you can detect both ability and dependence in it?
?
You cannot produce a single genuine reflection or inference without God's support. Does that realization humble you or make you restless? Are you willing to pause right now and admit that even your ability to read this question is a gift you did not earn? Write down one action you thought you did independently today, and then consciously acknowledge that it was sustained by God.
?
If every 'I can' is accompanied by 'I need', then your every achievement is also a confession of need. Will you let that confession change how you speak about your successes? In the next conversation where you receive praise, will you mentally or verbally redirect the credit to the One who made it possible?

Limits of Power: Evidence from Thought, Speech, and Action
Author provides empirical evidence of limited human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three concrete examples showing that human power is limited and dependent on God's creation.

Core Idea
We can initiate an act but cannot control its outcome or every detail.

After stating the general principle, the author provides three vivid examples to prove that human power is not absolute. Each example shows that while we can perform an action, we cannot fully control its results or the precise details. This demonstrates our need for divine enabling.

Guided Path
1
Read the three examples
Identify the three cases: reflection (naẓar) and resulting knowledge, tongue movement and articulation, hand movement and separate joints.
Each example follows the pattern: 'he feels power over X, but not over Y.'
2
Analyze the reflection example
We can choose to reflect, but once we reflect, we cannot prevent knowledge from occurring. This shows that knowledge is not entirely within our control.
3
Analyze the speech example
We can move our tongue to produce letters, but we cannot arbitrarily change the points of articulation or the sounds. The physical structure limits us.
4
Analyze the hand example
We can move our hand, but we cannot move a single finger joint without moving the connecting ligaments. The body's design imposes dependencies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحقيق ذلك من غير جد عن الإنصاف أن العبد كما يجس من نفسه التمكن من الفعل لوتيسر التأتي يحس من نفسه الافتقار والاحتياج إلى معين في كل ما يتصرف ويجد في استطاعته، ويتكلف فقدان الاستقلال والاستبداد بالفعل في كل ما يأتي ويذر ويقدم ويؤخر من تصرفات فكره نظراً واستدلالا، ومن حركات لسانه قيلا وقالا، ومن ترددات يديه يمينا وشمالا فيحس الاقتدار على النظر، ولا يحس الاقتدار على العلم بعد حصول النظر، فإنه لو أراد أن لا يحصل لا يتمكن منه ويحس من نفسه تحريك لسانه بالحروف ولو أراد أن يبدل المخارج ويغير الأصوات لم يتمكن من ذلك، ويحس تحريك يديه وأنملته، ولو أراد تحريك جزء واحد من غير تحريك الرباطات المتصلة لم يتمكن من ذلك، وعند الخصم القدرة صالحة للأضداد والأمثال وهي متشابهة في القادرين، فالعبد مستقل بالإيجاد والاختراع، وليس إلى الباري سبحانه وتعالى من هذه الأفعال إلا خلق القدرة فحسب واشتراط البنية من أضعف ما يتصور. والحق في المسألة تسليم التمكن والتأتي والاستطاعة على الفعل على وجه ينتسب إلى العبد وجه من الفعل يليق بصلاحية قدرته واستطاعته، وإثبات الافتقار والاحتياج، ونفي الاستقلال والاستبداد، فنجد في التكليف موردي الخطاب فعلا ويضاف في الجزاء مقابلة وتفضلا والله أعلم.
And the verification of that, without deviating from fairness, is that the servant, just as he feels from himself the ability (al-tamakkun) to do the act if the means are facilitated (law tayassara al-ta'tī), he feels from himself need (al-iftiqār) and dependence (al-iḥtiyāj) upon an aider in everything he performs, and he finds in his capacity (istitā‘atihi) and he perceives the lack of independence (al-istiqlāl) and self-sufficiency (al-istibdād) in every act he does and leaves, advances and delays, of the movements of his thought, reflection and inference, and of the movements of his tongue, speaking and saying, and of the movements of his hands, right and left. So he feels power (al-iqtidār) over reflection (al-naẓar), but he does not feel power over knowledge (al-‘ilm) after reflection occurs, for if he wanted it not to occur, he would not be able to do that. And he feels from himself moving his tongue with letters, but if he wanted to change the points of articulation and alter the sounds, he would not be able to do that. And he feels moving his hands and fingers, but if he wanted to move a single part without moving the connected ligaments, he would not be able to do that. And according to the opponent (al-khaṣm), power (al-qudra) is capable of opposites (al-aḍdād) and similars (al-amthāl), and they are similar among those who are powerful (al-qādirīn). So the servant is independent in bringing into existence (al-ījād) and invention (al-ikhtirā‘), and nothing is due to the Creator, exalted, from these acts except the creation of power only, and the condition of the physical constitution (al-bunya) is among the weakest of what can be imagined. And the truth (al-ḥaqq) in the issue is the affirmation of ability (al-tamakkun), facilitation (al-ta'tī), and capacity (al-istiṭā‘a) upon the act in a manner that the servant has a side of the act that befits the fitness of his power and capacity, and the affirmation of need (al-iftiqār) and dependence (al-iḥtiyāj), and the negation of independence (al-istiqlāl) and self-sufficiency (al-istibdād). So we find in obligation (al-taklīf) two sources of address (mūriday al-khiṭāb) as an act, and it is attributed in recompense (al-jazā') as a recompense and a favor. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a refutation-and-correct-statement concerning the nature of human action and divine power. The author begins by establishing empirical evidence: a person feels both ability (tamakkun) to act (if means are facilitated) and a sense of need (iftiqār) and dependence (iḥtiyāj) on a helper in every action. He perceives the lack of independence (istiqlāl) and self-sufficiency (istibdād) in all his acts—whether mental (reflection, inference), verbal (speaking), or physical (hand movements). Then the author introduces a specific contrast: one feels power (iqtidār) over the act of reflection, but not over the knowledge that results after reflection; if one wanted knowledge not to occur, one could not prevent it. Similarly, one can move his tongue to produce letters but cannot arbitrarily change the points of articulation; one can move his hand and fingers but cannot move a single joint without moving connected ligaments. This shows that human power is limited and dependent on God's enabling. The author then presents the opponent (al-khaṣm—the Mu'tazilite) who claims that power (qudra) is capable of opposites (aḍdād) and similars (amthāl) and that all powers are alike in all agents. According to that view, the servant is fully independent in creating and inventing (ījād wa ikhtirā‘), and God's role is merely to create that power and to provide the physical constitution (bunya). The author dismisses this as one of the weakest positions conceivable. Finally, the author states the correct view (al-ḥaqq): we affirm ability (tamakkun), facilitation (ta'tī), and capacity (istiṭā‘a) for the act in a way that the servant has a genuine side (wajh) of the act that befits the fitness of his power and capacity. At the same time, we affirm need (iftiqār) and dependence (iḥtiyāj), and we deny independence (istiqlāl) and self-sufficiency (istibdād). The result is that in obligation (taklīf) we have two sources of address (mūriday al-khiṭāb) for the act, and in recompense (jazā') it is attributed as a recompense and favor. The passage ends with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms
الاقتدار
النظر
العلم
المخارج
الرباطات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author say about knowledge (al-‘ilm) after reflection?
A) We have full power to produce knowledge.
B) We do not have power over its occurrence; it follows necessarily.
C) We can choose to have knowledge without reflection.
D) Knowledge is entirely independent of reflection.

Key Takeaway
Human power is real but always limited; complete control belongs only to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you take credit for outcomes, forgetting that the results are not under your control? Will you consciously acknowledge God's control after your efforts today?
?
You can move your tongue to speak, but you cannot change the very mechanism of speech. How does recognizing this limitation humble your sense of self-sufficiency? What specific thought or habit will you change because of it?
?
The passage shows you have power to reflect but not over the knowledge that comes after. When last did you assume your understanding was entirely your own doing? How can you thank God for the knowledge He grants, especially in matters of faith?

Refuting the Mu’tazilite View of Power
Author critiques the Mu'tazilite view of human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position on human power and why the author considers it weak.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites claim humans are independent creators; the author calls this one of the weakest views.

Having established the empirical evidence of limited human power, the author now presents the opposing view – that of the Mu'tazilites (al-khaṣm). He states their claims and then dismisses them as extremely weak. This contrast helps clarify the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's view
Identify what the Mu'tazilites affirm: power can produce opposites, powers are alike in all agents, and humans are independent in creation.
The term 'al-khaṣm' refers to the Mu'tazilite opponent.
2
Understand the reduction of God's role
Note that according to the opponent, God's role is limited to creating power and providing the physical constitution (bunya).
3
Appreciate the criticism
The author dismisses this as 'among the weakest of what can be imagined.' This is a strong rhetorical dismissal.
Why is it weak? Because it contradicts the empirical evidence just presented.

According to the Mu'tazilite view presented, what is God's role in human actions?
A) God creates everything directly, including human actions.
B) God only creates the power in humans and provides the physical constitution.
C) God has no role in human actions.
D) God creates human actions but not human power.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite view of human independence in action is rejected by the Ash'aris as extremely weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what moments of your daily life do you feel most self-sufficient, and how can you use those moments to pause and acknowledge that even your power to act is a gift from God?
?

?
The Mu'tazilite view is called 'among the weakest of what can be imagined' because it ignores human dependence. Where do you see traces of this same mistaken independence in your own heart, and what concrete habit could you start today to uproot it?

The Ash'ari Position: Affirmation and Negation
Author states the correct Ash'ari position on human action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct Ash'ari position: affirming human ability and God's sovereignty, and negating human independence.

Core Idea
The truth is to affirm ability while negating independence; God is the ultimate source.

After criticizing the Mu'tazilite view, the author states the correct position (al-ḥaqq). This is a balanced affirmation: humans have a genuine side in their actions (wajh) that fits their capacity, yet they remain in need of God. This reconciliation is key to understanding Islamic theology of action.

Guided Path
1
Read the correct position
The author affirms tamakkun, ta'tī, and istiṭā‘a (ability, facilitation, capacity) while also affirming iftiqār and iḥtiyāj (need, dependence), and negating istiqlāl and istibdād (independence, self-sufficiency).
2
Understand the 'wajh' (aspect) of the act
The servant has an aspect (wajh) of the act that befits his power. This shows the act is genuinely attributable to the servant, but not independently.
This is the Ash'ari concept of kasb (acquisition).
3
Connect to taklif and jaza
The last sentence mentions that in obligation (taklīf) there are two sources of address, and in recompense (jazā') the act is attributed as a recompense and favor. This shows the practical consequence.

Checkpoint
What does the author affirm about the servant's relation to the act?
A) The servant has no part in the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something good, do you feel a sense of self-sufficiency? How does this lesson force you to re-evaluate that feeling? Commit to consciously thanking God for the ability itself, not just the outcome.
?
Identify one area of your life where you habitually rely on your own planning and effort without feeling your need for God. What would it look like to act from this lesson—affirming your ability while negating independence?
?
The text says the act has a 'side' that belongs to you, yet God creates it. Does this make you less responsible for your sins, or more? Be honest: how do you use this to either excuse yourself or to strive harder? What will you change today?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
4 min
The Second Proof: Contingency and Infinite Regress
Sanusi presents a clearer proof from contingency and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the second proof from contingency and the impossibility of infinite regress, and how it refutes the notion of accidents subsisting in God's essence.

Core Idea
Every contingent needs a cause; infinite regress is impossible.

Sanusi presents a second proof, which he considers clearer than the first, to refute those who posit accidents in God's essence. The proof hinges on the principle that any contingent entity, regardless of where it subsists, requires an external determiner to bring it into existence. By showing that infinite regress is impossible, he establishes that contingency points to a necessary being, which cannot itself be contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once without analyzing. Let the argument flow naturally.
Notice that the passage builds from the nature of contingency to the need for a determiner.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms ḥādith, muḥdith, jāʾiz al-wujūd wa-l-ʿadam, murajjiḥ, and tasalsul in the Arabic text. Understand their meanings from the translation.
These terms form the backbone of the argument.
3
Trace the proof steps
Break the argument into premises: (1) every contingent is possible of existence and non-existence, (2) when it exists, it needs a determiner, (3) if that determiner is also contingent, it needs another determiner, leading to infinite regress. The impossibility of infinite regress implies a necessary determiner.
The commentary explains that this applies equally to accidents in God's essence.
4
Apply to the divine attributes
Consider the implication: if the opponent posits accidental attributes in God, those attributes would be contingent, thus requiring a determiner. This leads either to infinite regress or to a necessary being, which cannot be God if God is the locus of contingency.
The proof thus refutes the compatibility of real, contingent accidents with God's necessary existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان آخر أوضح مما قد سبق، وهو أن كل حادث يحتاج إلى محدث من حيث إنه كان في نفسه وباعتبار ذاته جائز الوجود والعدم، فلما ترجح جانب الوجود على العدم احتاج إلى مرجح بالضرورة، ثم ذلك المرجح إن كان حادثا احتاج إلى مرجح ثم يتسلسل القول فيه إلى ما لا نهاية له وجهة الاحتياج لا يختلف الحال فيها بين حادث في ذاته سبحانه وتعالى وبين محدث مباين ذاته، فإنه إنما احتاج بجهة
Another proof, clearer than what has preceded, and it is that every contingent (ḥādith) needs a producer (muḥdith) because it is in itself and by consideration of its essence possible of existence and non-existence. When the side of existence outweighs non-existence, it necessarily needs a determiner (murajjiḥ). Then that determiner, if it is contingent, needs a determiner, and then the discourse would go on in infinite regress (tasalsul). And the aspect of need does not differ in its state between a contingent in His essence, exalted, and a producer separate from His essence. For it only need by an aspect
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof, which the author says is clearer than the first. It is an argument from contingency and the impossibility of infinite regress. The proof begins: every contingent (ḥādith) requires a producer (muḥdith) because it is in its own essence (bi-iʿtibār dhātihī) possible (jāʾiz) of existence and non-existence. Since it came into existence rather than remaining non-existent, it requires a determiner (murajjiḥ) that tips the scale towards existence. That determiner, if itself contingent, would require another determiner, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) which is impossible. The crucial point is that the need for a cause—the aspect of contingency—does not differ whether the contingent entity is said to subsist in God's essence (e.g., an accident claimed to be in God) or is a separate created entity. In both cases, the entity is contingent by its own essence and thus requires a cause. Therefore, if one posits accidents in God's essence, they would be contingent and would need a cause, leading to an infinite regress or to a necessary cause which cannot be God because God would then be the locus of something that itself needs a cause. The argument effectively forces the opponent to accept either infinite regress or the impossibility of accidents subsisting in God.

Key Terms
حادث
محدث
جائز الوجود والعدم
مرجح
تسلسل
جهة الاحتياج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents in God's essence could be necessary by virtue of being in God, so they do not require a cause.
Correction: The proof shows that the contingency of an entity is based on its own essence, not its locus. Even if it subsists in God, its essence remains possible, so it still needs a determiner.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the second proof regarding accidents in God's essence?
A) Accidents cannot be in God because they are material.
B) If accidents were in God, they would be contingent and need a cause, leading to infinite regress.
C) God can only have one attribute at a time.
D) The determiner must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The second proof establishes that any contingent entity, regardless of its locus, requires an external determiner, and the impossibility of infinite regress necessitates a necessary being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that every contingent thing—including your very existence—depends entirely on an external determiner. When you look at your talents, relationships, or even your faith, do you truly feel their contingency, or do you secretly believe they are self-sufficient? What would it mean for you to live each moment conscious that you are being 'tipped' from non-existence into existence by God?
?
Sanusi argues that even if an attribute were in God, it would still need a determiner. Now consider the things you value most about yourself: your intelligence, your kindness, your spirituality. If these were considered 'yours' as attributes, are they contingent? Do you rely on them as if they were necessary? How does it change your reliance on God to admit that even your best qualities are created and sustained by Him alone?
?
The proof ends with the necessity of a being that is not contingent—a being that requires no cause. In your own life, you constantly chase causes for your happiness: achievements, people, possessions. But these are all contingent and need external support. Are you willing to stop the infinite regress in your own heart and rest solely in the Necessary Being, even if it means letting go of all other supports?

Refutation of Potentiality in God
Author refutes philosopher linking potentiality to God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosopher's argument about potentiality leads to attributing privative properties to God, and why the author refutes it by affirming Divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The philosopher's potentiality argument actually forces God into matter-like privation.

In this passage, the author presents a philosopher's argument that seems to logically force a material-like potentiality onto God's essence. But the author turns the tables by showing that this argument would attribute to God the very properties of hyle—privative natures—which are incompatible with the Necessary Existent. Let's trace the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall flow.
Notice the tight logical sequence.
2
Identify the philosopher's argument
The philosopher argues that any essence acquiring a new meaning must have had prior preparedness. Trace the steps: essence without meaning → meaning arises → preparedness → change to actuality → implies potentiality and actuality → that is hyle and form.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author points out that this argument attributes privative properties (like preparedness, which is non-existence) to God, and that God is transcendent above such limitations.
The refutation is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The Necessary Existent is free from possibility and non-existence, which are sources of evil. This underscores divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ألزم عليه الفلسفي إلزاما لا محيص له عنه، فقال: كل ذات لم يحدث فيها معنى ثم حدث فيها قبل الحدوث استعداد القبول وصلاحيته وقوته، ثم إذا حدث فيها القبول تبدل الاستعداد بالوجود والصلاحية بالحصول، والقوة بالفعل، ويلزم أن يكون في ذاته معنى ما بالقوة، ثم معنى ما بالفعل، وذلك بعينه هو الهيولي والصورة، وقد أثبتوا لله سبحانه وتعالى قبل خلق العالم خصايص الهيولي وهي طبايع عدمية، فإن الاستعداد والصلاحية عدم شيء من إثباته أن يكون شيئًا، وواجب الوجود لذاته منزه عن طبيعة الإمكان والعدم اللذان هما منبع الشر.
And the philosopher has been forced into a binding objection from which there is no escape, saying: Every essence in which no meaning has arisen, then meaning arises in it, before that arising there is a preparedness for acceptance, its suitability and potentiality; then when acceptance occurs in it, the preparedness changes into existence, suitability into attainment, and potentiality into actuality. Consequently, there must be in its essence some meaning in potentiality, then some meaning in actuality, and this is exactly hyle and form. They have attributed to God Most High before the creation of the world the properties of hyle, which are privative natures, for preparedness and suitability are the non-existence of something from the affirmation of which it becomes something. The Necessary Existent in itself is transcendent above the nature of possibility and non-existence, which are the source of evil.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents a philosopher's objection and the author's refutation. The philosopher argues that any essence that acquires a new meaning must have had a prior potentiality for it, implying that God before creation had potentiality, which leads to the notion that God has hyle (matter) and form—a view that philosophers attribute to God. The author refutes by stating that the philosophers themselves attribute to God the properties of hyle, which are privative (negative) natures, such as preparedness and suitability which are non-existence. The author concludes that the Necessary Existent is transcendent above possibility and non-existence, which are the source of evil. Thus, the philosopher's argument is invalid because it leads to absurdities.

Key Terms
هيولي
استعداد
صلاحية
قوة
فعل
واجب الوجود
إمكان
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that potentiality in God is a valid concept that explains His ability to create.
Correction: The author shows that attributing potentiality to God would reduce Him to having matter-like privation (non-existence), which is impossible for the Necessary Existent.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim the philosopher's argument attributes to God?
A) The properties of hyle, which are privative natures.
B) Eternal will.
C) All-power.
D) Speech.

Key Takeaway
The philosopher's argument is invalid because it forces privative properties onto God, who is absolutely free from potentiality and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that potentiality and non-existence cannot be attributed to God. But consider: In your own life, do you ever unconsciously imagine God as 'waiting' for something to happen, as if He had a potentiality to respond? How does this lesson challenge that subtle anthropomorphism, and what step will you take today to affirm His absolute transcendence in your thoughts?
?
The philosopher's error was to impose a general principle about created essences onto the Creator. When you reflect on your own assumptions about God — especially when you try to 'figure out' how He acts — are you perhaps measuring Him by the standards of creatures? This lesson demands that you stop and repent of any such analogy. What specific mental habit will you abandon after reading this?
?
If preparedness and suitability are really privations, then before creation, God had no 'readiness' because He lacks nothing. Does your heart truly rest in a God who needs no preparation, no potentiality, no improvement? Or do you still rely on a god you can 'predict'? Resolve to worship the Necessary Existent as He is, not as your mind expects Him to be.

First Type of Ta'til: Materialist View and Refutation
Author refutes materialist denial of Creator by showing their own admission of a Maker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first type of ta'til (denying the Creator), the materialist claim of chance formation, and the author's refutation that even materialists acknowledge a Creator.

Core Idea
Even deniers of creation admit a Creator, making ta'til self-contradictory.

The author begins addressing the first type of ta'til: outright denial that the world has a Creator. He notes that no serious thinker holds this view except a small group of materialists who claim the world formed by chance collisions of atoms. However, he then points out that even these materialists do not actually deny a Creator; they merely attribute the world's formation to chance while still acknowledging a Maker. This subtle refutation exposes the incoherence of their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for the author's style. Then read the translation carefully to understand the claim and refutation.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the materialist claim
The materialists (dahriyya) say the world began as scattered atoms moving randomly, which collided by chance to form the universe. This is a denial of a purposeful Creator.
The key term 'اصطكاك' means collision.
3
Understand the refutation
The author argues that these materialists are not truly denying the Creator; they still acknowledge a Maker, but they ascribe the world's existence to chance as a precaution. This shows inconsistency.
The refutation is not that they are wrong, but that they contradict themselves.
4
Reflect on the implications
This shows that even avowed materialists cannot fully escape belief in a Creator, supporting the kalam argument that denial of creation is rationally untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما تعطيل العالم عن الصانع العالم القادر الحكيم فلست أراها مقالة لأحد، ولا أعرف عليه صاحب مقالة إلا ما نُقل عن شرذمة قليلة من الدهرية أنهم قالوا : العالم كان في الأزل أجزاء مبثوثة تتحرك على غير استقامة واصطكت اتفاقا، فحصل عنها العالم بشكله الذي تراه عليه ودارت الأكوار وكرت الأدوار وحدثت المركبات ولست أرى صاحب هذه المقالة ممن ينكر الصانع، بل هو معترف بالصانع، لكنه يحيل سبب وجود العالم على البحث والاتفاق احترازا
As for ta'til of the world from the Maker, the Knowing, the Powerful, the Wise, I do not see that as a claim of anyone, nor do I know any proponent of it except what is narrated from a small group of materialists (dahriyya) who said: the world was in pre-eternity scattered parts moving without order and collided by chance, and from them resulted the world in its form that you see, and the cycles revolved and the ages repeated and the compounds came into being. And I do not see the proponent of this claim as one who denies the Maker; rather he acknowledges the Maker, but he ascribes the cause of the world's existence to chance and agreement as a precaution.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage discusses the first type of ta'til: denying that the world has a Creator. The author asserts that he does not know anyone who actually holds this view, except a small group of materialists (dahriyya) who claim that the world was originally scattered atoms moving randomly and accidentally collided, forming the world we see. However, the author argues that even these materialists are not actually denying the Creator; rather, they acknowledge a Creator but attribute the cause of the world's existence to chance and agreement. This is a subtle refutation: although they appear to deny the Creator, they still admit a Creator, making their position inconsistent.

Key Terms

اتفاق
اصطكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Materialists (dahriyya) are complete atheists who deny any Creator.
Correction: The author explicitly states that even these materialists acknowledge a Maker, but they claim the world formed by chance as a precaution. Thus, they are not true atheists.

Checkpoint

A) They completely deny the Creator
B) They acknowledge a Creator but ascribe the world's formation to chance
C) They are undecided
D) They claim the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
The first type of ta'til (denying the Creator) is not genuinely held by anyone; even materialists admit a Creator, revealing their position's inconsistency.

Why God Cannot Be Called a Cause
Refutes calling God a cause with two impossible consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical kalam theologians rejected describing God as a 'cause' (علة) and the two impossible consequences that follow from such a description.

Core Idea
Calling God a cause implies subordination and necessity, which contradict His absolute sovereignty.

Theologians sometimes debated whether causation applies to God. In this passage, the author argues forcefully that calling God a 'cause' leads to absurdities. He lists two specific impossibilities that follow from such language. This refutation is presented as a crushing objection with no answer. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note the key terms: علة, مبدع, موجب, مناسبة, معلول.
2
Identify the two impossibilities
The passage explicitly mentions 'two impossible matters'. Find each one and understand the author's reasoning for why they are impossible.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends by saying this is a crushing criticism with no answer. Think about why the opponents cannot respond.
This connects to the principle that the higher does not act for the sake of the lower.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسميتم معبودكم علة ومبدعا وموجبا، وذلك يؤدي إلى أمرين محالين: أحدهما بثبوت المناسبة بين العلة والمعلول، وقد سبق تقريره، والثاني أن العلة توجب معلولها لذاتها، والقصد الأول وجود العالم من لوازم وجوده بالقصد الأول، فإن العالي لا يريد أمرا لأجل الأسفل فبطل أيضا التعليل، وهذا من الإلزامات المفحمة التي لا جواب عنها.
And you have named your worshipped object a cause, an originator, and a necessitator, and that leads to two impossible matters: one of them is the establishment of suitability between cause and effect, and its proof has preceded; and the second is that the cause necessitates its effect by its essence, and the primary intention is the existence of the world from the necessities of its existence by primary intention, for the higher does not desire a thing for the sake of the lower; so the causal explanation is also invalidated. And this is among the crushing criticisms that have no answer.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The author attacks those who call God a cause (علة), originator (مبدع), or necessitator (موجب). He argues that such terminology leads to two impossibilities. First, it implies a relation of suitability (مناسبة) between cause and effect, which the author claims has already been disproven. Second, it implies that the cause necessitates its effect by its essence, meaning that the world's existence is a necessary consequence of God's existence as a primary intention—but according to the author, the higher (God) does not act for the sake of the lower (creation). Therefore, causal explanation fails. The author concludes that this is a devastating objection with no answer.

Key Terms
علة
مبدع
موجب
مناسبة
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author denies any relation between God and creation.
Correction: The author denies only the specific causal relation that implies equivalence or necessitation, not all relation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what are the two impossible consequences of calling God a cause?
A) 1) Establishing suitability between cause and effect, 2) The cause necessitates its effect by essence.
B) 1) God would be composite, 2) The world would be eternal.
C) 1) Denying divine attributes, 2) Affirming multiplicity in God.
D) 1) God would be subject to change, 2) The world would be created from nothing.

Key Takeaway
Describing God as a cause leads to the impossible conclusions of suitability and necessitation, thus such language is rejected in kalam.

The Absurdity of Distinguishing by Existence Instead of Occupancy
The author shows the logical consequences if distinction were by existence rather than occupancy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why distinguishing substance and accident by existence rather than occupancy leads to logical contradictions and undermines rational thought.

Core Idea
Distinguishing by existence collapses all differences because existence is common to all.

The author argues that if substance and accident were distinguished by existence, since existence is common to both, the basis of distinction would be the same as the basis of sharing. This leads to absurd consequences: similarity and difference vanish, reasoning becomes impossible, and even sense perception becomes fragmented. This lesson explores the logical trap of using existence as a differentiator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the author's use of 'law' (if) to set up a hypothetical.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the reductio structure
The author assumes the false premise (distinction by existence) and shows it leads to contradictions. Identify the premise and the chain of conclusions.
The structure is: if P, then Q, and Q is absurd, so not P.
3
List the absurd consequences
The passage enumerates several consequences: elimination of similarity/difference/opposition, failure of analogical reasoning, failure of negation of opposites, and breakdown of reason and sense.
The consequences escalate in severity.
4
Grasp the deeper point
Understand why this argument proves that occupancy (taḥayyuz) is the correct differentiator. Reflect on how this upholds rational thought.
This reinforces the necessity of specific properties for distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أن الجوهر مايز العرض بوجوده كما مايزه بتحيزه؛ لحكم على العرض بأنه متحيز، وعلى الجوهر بأنه محتاج إلى التحيز؛ لأن الوجود والتحيز واحد، فما به يفترقان هو بعينه ما فيه يشتركان، وما به يتماثلان هو بعينه ما فيه يختلفان، ويرتفع التماثل والاختلاف والتضاد رأسا. ويلزم أن لا يجري حكم مثل في مثل، حتى لو قام الدليل على حدوث جوهر بعينه كان محدثا، هو ويحتاج إلى دليل آخر في مثل ذلك الجوهر. ويلزم أن لا يسلب حكم ضد عن ضد، وخلاف عن خلاف، حتى لو حكم على الجوهر بأنه قابل للعرض لم يسلب هذا الحكم عن العرض؛ إذ لا تماثل ولا اختلاف ولا تضاد، وارتفع العقل والمعقول، وتعدد الحس والمحسوس.
And if substance were distinguished from accident by its existence, just as it is distinguished by its occupancy, then it would be judged that accident is possessed of occupancy, and that substance is in need of occupancy; because existence and occupancy are one. So that by which they differ is exactly that in which they share, and that by which they are similar is exactly that in which they differ. And similarity, difference, and opposition would be utterly eliminated. And it would follow that no judgment of a like would apply to a like, even if proof were established for the origination of a specific substance, it would be originated, but it would need another proof for another similar substance. And it would follow that the judgment of a contrary would not be negated from a contrary, nor of a different from a different, until even if it were judged that substance is receptive to accident, this judgment would not be negated from accident; for there is no similarity, no difference, no opposition. And reason and the rational would be eliminated, and sense and the sensible would become multiple.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum of the idea that substance and accident are distinguished by existence rather than occupancy. The author argues that if they were distinguished by existence, then since existence is common to both, the specific difference would be exactly the same as the common property, leading to the collapse of all distinctions. Specifically, similarity, difference, and opposition would disappear. Consequently, no general judgment could be applied in reasoning: a proof for the origination of one substance would not apply to another, and even the basic property of substance (being receptive to accident) could not be denied of accident. Ultimately, reason and the rational would be nullified, and sense perception would become fragmented. This demonstrates the necessity of distinguishing by specific properties like occupancy, not by existence.

Key Terms
وجود (wujūd)
تحيز (taḥayyuz)
تماثل (tamāthul)
اختلاف (ikhtilāf)
تضاد (taḍādd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence can serve as a good differentiator between substance and accident because it is a fundamental attribute.
Correction: Existence is common to both substance and accident; using it as a differentiator leads to logical chaos, as shown by the reductio. The correct differentiator is occupancy (taḥayyuz), which is specific to substance.

Checkpoint
What would happen if substance were distinguished from accident by existence?
A) They would be clearly distinct
B) Similarity and difference would vanish
C) Substance would become accident
D) Existence would no longer be common

Key Takeaway
Distinguishing substance and accident by existence leads to the elimination of all rational distinctions and the nullification of reason itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the attributes of Allah, do you ever confuse what is true of Him alone with what is common to all creation? How does this lesson's warning about collapsing distinctions apply to the way you think about Allah's names and attributes?
?
The argument shows that using a shared property to distinguish leads to absurdity. In your own life, are you using something that is common to many (like wealth, status, or nationality) to distinguish between truth and falsehood, or to judge people? What would the lesson's logic say about that?
?
Reason itself is at stake if we make a wrong distinction at the foundation. After seeing how one flawed premise undoes all rational categories, what step in your own understanding of tawhid are you willing to re-examine to avoid a similar collapse?

The Pre-Existence of Essences for Creation
Author argues that distinct essences must pre-exist for intentional creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the realities of genera and species must be distinct and pre-existing for intentional creation, and that they are not themselves created.

Core Idea
Essences are not created; they are pre-existing distinct realities that make creation purposeful.

This passage presents a foundational argument in kalam about the nature of creation. The author asserts that for God to intentionally create specific things, those things must have distinct essences that are not themselves brought into being by the act of creation. Without these pre-existing distinctions, creation would be a matter of chance rather than intention.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic text once to get a sense of its rhythm and structure. Don't worry about understanding every word immediately.
Focus on key terms like جوهر, عرض, and حقيقة.
2
Follow the conditional argument
Notice how the author uses a conditional 'if...then' structure: if substance and accident were not distinct in non-existence, then intentional creation would be impossible.
The argument hinges on the necessity of pre-existing distinction for specification.
3
Consider the examples
The author gives examples like motion vs. rest, whiteness vs. blackness. See how these illustrate the need for distinct realities.
These examples show that without distinction, any outcome could occur.
4
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is that the realities of genera and species do not depend on the agent's act, and if they were not distinct, creation would be random.
This is a key point in the realist view of essences in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول إن الفاعل إذا أراد إيجاد جوهر فلا بد أن يتميز الجوهر بحقيقته عن العرض حتى يتحقق القصد إليه بالإيجاد وإلا فالجوهر والعرض في العدم إذا أحدهما عن الثاني بأمر ما وحقيقة ما وذلك الأمر والحقيقة لم يكن شيئا ثابتا لم يتجرد القصد إلى الجوهر دون العرض وإلى الحركة دون السكون والبياض دون السواد إلى غير ذلك والتخصيص بالوجود إنما يتصور إذا كان المخصص معينا مميزا عند المخصص حتى لا يقع جوهر بدل عرض ولا حركة بدل سكون ولا بياض بدل سواد فعلم بذلك أن حقائق الأجناس والأنواع لا تتعلق بفعل الفاعل وأنها في ذواتها إن لم تكن أشياء منفصلة لم يتصور الإيجاد والاختراع ولكان حصول الكائنات على اختلافها اتفاقا وبختا.
And we say: When the agent wants to bring a substance into existence, it is necessary that the substance be distinguished by its reality from the accident, so that intention towards it through bringing into existence is realized. Otherwise, substance and accident, when in non-existence, if one of them is [distinguished] from the other by some matter and reality, and that matter and reality was not an established thing, then the intention would not be stripped towards the substance without the accident, nor towards motion without rest, nor towards whiteness without blackness, and so on. And specification by existence is only conceivable if the specified thing is determinate and distinct for the specifier, so that a substance does not occur in place of an accident, nor motion in place of rest, nor whiteness in place of blackness. So it is known by this that the realities of genera and species do not depend on the act of the agent, and that they in themselves, if they were not distinct things, then bringing into existence and invention would be inconceivable, and the occurrence of created things in their diversity would be by chance and luck.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the realities (حقائق) of genera and species are not brought into being by the agent's act; rather, they are distinct and pre-existing in themselves. The author argues that if an agent wants to create a substance, the substance must be inherently distinct from an accident by its very reality (حقيقته). Otherwise, if there were no such distinction when both are in non-existence, the agent could not intend specifically a substance rather than an accident, motion rather than rest, etc. Specification (تخصيص) of existence to a particular thing is only possible if the thing to be specified is already determinate and distinct (معين مميز) in the agent's knowledge. Without this pre-existing distinction, creation would be random—a substance could occur where an accident was intended, etc. Therefore, the realities of genera and species are not dependent on the agent's action; they are distinct things in themselves. If they were not distinct, then bringing into existence and invention (الإيجاد والاختراع) would be impossible, and the diversity of created things would be purely by chance and luck (اتفاقا وبختا). This argues for a realist view of essences that are not created by God, only their existence is given.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
حقيقة
تخصيص
إيجاد
حقائق الأجناس والأنواع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God creates both the essence and existence of things.
Correction: The essences (realities of genera and species) are distinct and pre-exist in non-existence; God only brings them into existence, not their essence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that realities of genera and species are not dependent on the agent's act?
A) Because they are created at the same time as existence
B) Because they must be distinct in non-existence for intentional creation to occur
C) Because the agent cannot know them
D) Because they are accidents

Key Takeaway
For intentional creation to be possible, the essences of things must be pre-existing and distinct, otherwise creation would be random.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life have you been living as if your identity is something you invent for yourself, rather than a distinct reality that God knows and intends? What would change if you truly believed that your essence is already distinct in His knowledge?
?
When was the last time you called something a 'coincidence' or 'luck'? Based on this passage, can you point to one such event and rewrite it as part of God's intentional creation? What does that demand of your faith?
?
If the diversity of creation is not by chance but by pre-existing distinct essences, then your purpose is not random. What specific step can you take today to live as if your existence is intended—not by blind fate, but by a personal God who knew you before you were?

The Identity of Substance: Absolute vs. Particular
Objection and response about the identity of a substance as 'this'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a particular substance cannot be identical to an absolute substance and the kalām response that the absolute as such is not 'this' particular.

Core Idea
An absolute substance cannot become a particular 'this'.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument about the identity of substances. The objector claims that if something was a substance, it must be indicated as 'this' particular. The response shows the impossibility of an absolute substance becoming a particular. Let us examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure.
2
Identify the objection
The objector says if something is a substance, it must be indicated by 'this'. Understand why they think that.
3
Trace the response
The response argues that if before existence it was absolute, it was not 'this'; therefore the particular 'this' cannot be that absolute.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that what is established in non-existence never gains existence, and what gains existence was not previously established. Consider the implications for kalām theories of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلتم: كان بعينه جوهرًا فيجب أن تحقق الإشارة إليه بهذا، ويكون ذلك المشار إليه هو هذا؛ لأن هذا لا يشاركه فيه غير هذا، وإن كان قبل وجوده جوهراً مطلقاً لا هذا فلم يكن ذلك هذا، فلم يكن هذا شيئًا، والمطلق من حيث هو مطلق لا هذا، ولم يكن هذا ذاك ولا ذاك هذا، فما هو ثابت في العدم لم يتحقق له وجود، وما تحقق له وجود لم يكن ثابتا.
For if you say: It was itself a substance, then it must be that the indication to it is by 'this', and that indicated is 'this'; because 'this' is not shared by other than this, and if before its existence it was an absolute substance not this, then that was not this, so this was not a thing, and the absolute insofar as it is absolute is not this, and neither was this that nor that this, so that which is established in non-existence has no existence realized for it, and that which has existence realized for it was not established.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response concerning the identity of a substance. The objector claims that if something was itself a substance, then it must be indicated by 'this' – meaning a particular entity. The response argues that if before existence it was an absolute substance (not this particular one), then it was not this, so the particular 'this' would not be a thing. The absolute qua absolute is not 'this', and the particular cannot be the absolute. Therefore, what is established in non-existence never gets existence, and what gets existence was not previously established. This argument refutes the notion that a substance pre-exists as an absolute and then becomes particular.

Key Terms
جوهر (substance)
مطلق (absolute)
معين (particular)
إشارة (indication)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies the existence of universals or that a particular can share in a nature.
Correction: The argument does not deny universals; it denies that a particular substance can be identical to an absolute substance, because the absolute as such is not 'this'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot an absolute substance become a particular 'this'?
A) Because the absolute is not a thing at all.
B) Because the absolute, insofar as it is absolute, is not 'this'.
C) Because 'this' is shared by many substances.
D) Because the absolute can become particular by acquiring existence.

Key Takeaway
An absolute substance cannot become a particular 'this', so what is established in non-existence cannot come into existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

But this lesson forces you to see that your existence is not a mere instance of an absolute; you are this particular person, with this specific history and deeds. When you stand before Allah, will you be judged as a type or as this unique individual? How does that change how you prepare for that Meeting?
?
The argument says what is established in non-existence never gets existence. What 'non-existent' person do you imagine yourself to be—an ideal version in your mind that you have not yet realized? Are you delaying your real, particular existence by clinging to an absolute fantasy?
?
If the absolute cannot become the particular, then your faith cannot remain abstract; it must be actualized in this moment, in this action. What is one specific, concrete deed you will do today that makes your faith particular, not just absolute?
