Essential Priority: The Foundation of Cause and Condition
The author establishes essential priority as the criterion distinguishing cause from condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how essential priority (taqaddum bi al-dhat) distinguishes cause from condition, even when they coexist temporally.

Core Idea
Cause deserves essential priority over effect; condition does not.

The author begins by asserting a critical principle: anyone who uses causal or conditional language must accept a real priority in essence, not just in time. This priority is what allows us to say, for example, that knowledge makes a knower, not the reverse. In this lesson, we will explore this foundational idea and see it applied to three key examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on essential priority
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the author's claim that cause deserves priority in essence over effect, even if they coexist.
Focus on the phrase 'التقدم بالذات' (priority in essence).
2
Identify the three examples
The author gives examples: knowledge and the knower, ability and the act, and life as condition for knowledge. For each, note how the distinction relies on essential priority.
In each case, one term is prior in essence to the other.
3
Reflect on the universal principle
Ask yourself why this distinction matters for theology. If cause and effect were identical in essence, causal language would collapse. This principle underlies many arguments in kalam.
Consider how this relates to discussions of divine attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن يلزم على كل من قال بالعلة والمعلول والشرط والمشروط سؤال التقدم والتأخر بالذات، وإن كانا متلازمين في الوجود فإن العلة إنما صارت مقتضية للحكم لاستحقاقها التقدم عليه بذاته، والمعلول إنما صار مقتضيا للعلة لاستحقاقه التأخر عنها بذاته، وبهذا أمكنك أن تقول : إنما صار العالم عالما لقيام العلم به، ولا يمكنك أن تقول : إنما قام العلم به لكونه عالما، ولو كان حكمهما في الذات حكما واحدا لم يثبت هذا الفرق ويمثل هذا نفرق بين القدرة الحادثة والمقدور، فإن الاستطاعة وإن كانت مع الفعل وجودا إلا أنها قبل الفعل ذاتا واستحقاق وجود، ولهذا أمكنك أن تقول : حصل الفعل بالاستطاعة ولا يمكنك أن تقول : حصلت الاستطاعة بالفعل، وهذا قولنا في الشرط والمشروط، فإن المحل يجب أن يكون حيا حتى يقوم العلم به والقدرة، ولا يمكنك أن تقول : العلم والقدرة حتى يكون حيا وإلا فيرتفع التميز بين الشرط والمشروط. ولا تظنن أن هذا الفرق راجع إلى مجرد اللفظ، فإن التفرقة المذكورة قضية عقلية وراء اللفظ، ولا تظنن أيضًا أنه راجع إلى تقديرنا الوهمي؛ حيث يقدر استحقاق وجود لأحدهما دون الثاني، وافتقار وجود أحدهما بالثاني، فإن كل تقدير وهمي إذا لم يكن معقولاً أمكن تبديله بغيره من التقديرات، وهذا لا يمكن تبديله بل هذه قضية معقولة، فيلزم على ذلك أحد أمرين؛ إما أن يترك القول بالعلة والمعلول والشرط والمشروط رأسًا، ولا يطلق لفظ الإيجاب والاقتضاء على المعاني، بل يقال : معنى كون الشيء عالما قيام العلم به، ومعنى قيام العلم به اتصاف محله به من غير فرق، لكن يشتق له اسم من العلم فيقال عالم كما يشتق له اسم من الفعل فيقال : فاعل، وهذا أمر راجع إلى اللغة فحسب، فلا اقتضاء ولا إيجاب ولا علة ولا معلول، وهذا أهون الأمرين. وإما أن يقضي بسبق العلم على العالمية، وأن يكون الوجود بالعلم أولا وأولى منه بالعالمية، ثم يحكم بسبق الذات على الصفات، وأن يكون الوجود
But upon everyone who speaks of cause and effect and condition and conditioned, the question of priority and posteriority in essence is incumbent, even if they are concomitant in existence. For the cause only becomes necessitating of the judgment due to its deserving priority over it in essence, and the effect only becomes necessitating of the cause due to its deserving posteriority to it in essence. And by this you can say: 'The world only became knowledgeable because of knowledge subsisting in it,' and you cannot say: 'Knowledge only subsisted in it because it is knowledgeable.' And if their judgment in essence were one, this difference would not be established. And we distinguish similarly between the temporally originated power and the empowered act: for ability, although it is with the act in existence, is before the act in essence and in deserving existence. Therefore you can say: 'The act occurred through ability,' and you cannot say: 'Ability occurred through the act.' And this is our statement concerning condition and conditioned: the locus must be alive so that knowledge and power subsist in it, and you cannot say: 'Knowledge and power [subsist] so that it is alive,' otherwise the distinction between condition and conditioned would be nullified. And do not think that this difference is merely verbal; for the mentioned distinction is an intellectual judgment beyond the verbal. And do not also think that it is due to our imaginary estimation, where existence is estimated as deserved for one without the other, and the existence of one is dependent on the second. For every imaginary estimation, if not reasonable, can be replaced by another estimation, but this cannot be replaced; rather it is an intelligible judgment. Therefore one of two things is incumbent upon this: either to abandon entirely the talk of cause and effect and condition and conditioned, and not to use the words 'necessitation' and 'implication' for meanings, but rather to say: 'The meaning of a thing being knowledgeable is the subsistence of knowledge in it,' and 'The meaning of the subsistence of knowledge in it is the description of its locus by it without any difference,' but a name is derived for it from knowledge, so it is called 'knowing,' just as a name is derived for it from the act, so it is called 'agent.' And this is merely a matter of language: no necessitation, no implication, no cause, no effect. And this is the easier of the two things. Or to judge that knowledge precedes being knowledgeable, and that existence through knowledge is first and more prior than through being knowledgeable, then to judge that the essence precedes the attributes, and that existence [is through essence].
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the essential distinction between cause ('illa) and condition (shart). The author asserts that anyone who posits cause-effect or condition-conditioned relationships must accept that there is a priority in essence (taqaddum bi al-dhat) even if the two are temporally concomitant. For example, the cause deserves priority over its effect in essence, and the effect deserves posteriority. This is demonstrated by the fact that we can say 'the world became knowledgeable because knowledge subsists in it' but not the reverse; the world's being knowledgeable depends on knowledge, not vice versa. Similarly, ability (istita'a) is prior in essence to the act, even though they occur together in time. The same applies to condition and conditioned: the locus must be alive for knowledge to subsist, not the opposite. The author then warns that this distinction is neither merely verbal nor imaginary; it is a rational judgment (qadiyya 'aqliyya). He then presents two alternatives: either abandon causal language entirely and treat names like 'knowing' and 'agent' as purely derivative linguistic terms, which is easier; or accept that knowledge is prior to being knowledgeable, and essence is prior to attributes. The second alternative is left incomplete in the text, but it implies a metaphysical commitment to essential priority.

Key Terms
علة ('illa)
معلول (ma'lul)
شرط (shart)
مشروط (mashrut)
تقدم بالذات (taqaddum bi al-dhat)
تأخر بالذات (ta'akhkhur bi al-dhat)
قدرة (qudra)
مقدور (maqdur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that cause and condition are identical because they can coexist temporally.
Correction: Essential priority shows cause is prior in essence, not just time; condition is not prior in the same way.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes cause from condition?
A) Cause always exists before the effect in time.
B) Cause deserves priority in essence over the effect, even if they coexist.
C) Condition is always imaginary while cause is real.
D) There is no real distinction; it is merely verbal.

Key Takeaway
Essential priority (taqaddum bi al-dhat) is the rational basis for distinguishing cause from condition, even when they are temporally concomitant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What does it mean for Allah to be essentially prior to everything you do? Have you truly given Him that priority in your intentions, or do you merely acknowledge it in time after the fact?
?
If ability is essentially before the act, how does that reshape your understanding of your own responsibility? Do you see your choices as flowing from a prior capacity granted by Allah, or do you take that capacity for granted?
?
When you think about your own life, what condition (like life itself) are you taking as a given, forgetting that it is essentially prior to everything you do? How can you live today with that awareness?

The Rational Distinction and Two Theological Alternatives
The author defends the rational basis of the distinction and offers two alternatives for theologians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the essential priority distinction is rational (not verbal or imaginary) and the two theological alternatives it leads to.

Core Idea
The distinction is a rational judgment, not a linguistic or imaginary one.

After establishing the essential priority distinction, the author addresses two potential misunderstandings: that the distinction is merely verbal or merely imaginary. He insists it is a real intellectual judgment. He then presents two stark alternatives for theologians: either abandon causal language entirely (the easier path) or affirm that knowledge precedes being knowledgeable and essence precedes attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the rational nature of the distinction
Read the Arabic and translation from 'ولا تظنن' to the end. Note the author's rejection of verbal and imaginary interpretations.
The phrase 'قضية عقلية' (intellectual judgment) is key.
2
Analyze the two alternatives
List the two alternatives: (1) abandon causal terms and treat names as purely linguistic, or (2) accept priority of knowledge over being knowledgeable and essence over attributes.
Consider why the first is 'easier' and the second is more committed.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself which alternative aligns with mainstream kalam. How does this passage connect to discussions of divine attributes?
The second alternative implies a real distinction between essence and attributes.

The Rational Distinction Between Condition and Conditioned
Distinguishing rational priority between condition and conditioned with two consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational basis for distinguishing between condition and conditioned, and the two doctrinal consequences that follow from affirming or denying this distinction.

Core Idea
The condition-conditioned distinction is a rational judgment, not a verbal game.

This passage tackles a fundamental question in Islamic theology and philosophy: is the distinction between a condition and its conditioned merely a matter of language or imagination, or does it reflect a real rational priority? The author, using the example of capability (istita'ah) and the act, argues for the latter and then presents two stark alternatives that follow from accepting or rejecting this rational distinction. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated emphasis on 'rational judgment'.
2
Understand the example of capability and act
Focus on the relationship between capability (istita'ah) and the act. The author says capability is prior in essence and deservingness, even though both coexist in existence.
This exemplifies the condition-conditioned relationship.
3
Grasp the rational nature of the distinction
The author insists this is a rational judgment (qadiyya 'aqliyya), not a verbal or imaginary distinction. He argues that imaginary estimations can be replaced, but this cannot.
This is key to the argument.
4
Identify the two consequences
The author presents two possible outcomes: (1) abandon causality language entirely, or (2) affirm a problematic priority of knowledge over being known and essence over attributes.
The first is easier, the second more repugnant.
5
Reflect on the author's stance
The author leaves the choice to God, indicating the difficulty of the issue. Reflect on why both options are problematic.
This shows the depth of the theological problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن تقول : إنما قام العلم به لكونه عالما ، ولو كان حكمهما في الذات حكمًا واحدًا لم يثبت هذا الفرق ويمثل هذا نفرق بين القدرة الحادثة والمقدور، فإن الاستطاعة وإن كانت مع الفعل وجودًا إلا أنها قبل الفعل وجودًا إلا أنها قبل الفعل ذاتا واستحقاق وجود، ولهذا أمكنك أن تقول : حصل الفعل بالاستطاعة ولا يمكنك أن تقول : حصلت الاستطاعة بالفعل ، وهذا قولنا في الشرط والمشروط ، فإن المحل يجب أن يكون حيا حتى يقوم العلم به والقدرة، أولا يمكنك أن تقول : العلم والقدرة أولا حتى يكون حيا وإلا فيرتفع التميز بين الشرط والمشروط.

ولا تظنن أن هذا الفرق راجع إلى مجرد اللفظ، فإن التفرقة المذكورة قضية عقلية وراء اللفظ، ولا تظنن أيضًا أنه راجع إلى تقديرنا الوهمي ؛ حيث يقدر استحقاق وجود لأحدهما دون الثاني، وافتقار وجود أحدهما بالثاني، فإن كل تقدير وهمي إذا لم يكن معقولاً أمكن تبديله بغيره من التقديرات ، لو هذا لا يمكن تبديله بل هذه قضية معقولة، فيلزم على ذلك أحد أمرين ؛ إما أن يترك القول بالعلة والمعلول والشرط والمشروط رأسًا، ولا يطلق لفظ الإيجاب والاقتضاء على المعاني، بل يقال : معنى كون الشيء عالماً قيام العلم به، ومعنى قيام العلم به اتصاف محله به من غير فرق، لكن يشتق له اسم من العلم فيقال عالم كما يشتق له اسم من الفعل فيقال : فاعل ، وهذا أمر راجع إلى اللغة فحسب، فلا اقتضاء ولا إيجاب ولا علة ولا معلول، وهذا أهون الأمرين.

وإما أن يقضي بسبق العلم على العالمية، وأن يكون الوجود بالعلم أولا وأولى منه بالعالمية، ثم يحكم بسبق الذات على الصفات، وأن يكون الوجود بالذات أولا أولى منه بالصفات من حيث إن الذات قائمة بذاتها والصفات قائمة بها، حتى يكون الموصوف من حيث الذات أسبق على الصفة، والصفة من حيث الإيجاب والاقتضاء أسبق على الموصوف، وهذا أشنع الأمرين، والاستخارة إلى الله سبحانه وتعالى، وهو خير مخير
That you say: Knowledge subsists in it only because it is knowing, and if their judgment in the essence were one judgment, this difference would not be established. And this is exemplified by the difference between the created power and the thing powered, for capability (al-istita'ah), although it is with the act in existence, yet before the act it is in essence and deserving of existence. Hence you can say: the act occurs by capability, and you cannot say: capability occurs by the act. And this is our statement concerning the condition (al-shart) and the conditioned (al-mashrut). For the locus must be living for knowledge and power to subsist in it, and you cannot say: knowledge and power first so that it becomes living, otherwise the distinction between condition and conditioned is lost.

And do not think that this difference goes back to mere wording, for the mentioned distinction is a rational judgment beyond wording. And do not also think that it goes back to our imaginary estimation, where the deservingness of existence for one and not the other is estimated, and the dependence of existence of one on the other. For every imaginary estimation, if it is not rational, can be replaced by other estimations. But this cannot be replaced; rather it is a rational judgment. Therefore, one of two things necessarily follows: either to abandon the statement of cause (al-'illah) and effect (al-ma'lul) and condition and conditioned entirely, and not apply the words of necessitation (al-ijab) and requirement (al-iqtida') to meanings, but rather to say: the meaning of something's being knowing is the subsistence of knowledge in it, and the meaning of the subsistence of knowledge in it is the description of its locus by it without difference, but a name is derived for it from knowledge so it is called knowing, just as a name is derived for it from the act so it is called agent. And this is a matter referring to language alone, so there is no necessitation, no requirement, no cause, no effect. And this is the easier of the two matters.

Or to judge that knowledge precedes 'being knowing', and that existence by knowledge is prior and more fitting than by being knowing, then to judge that the essence precedes the attributes, and that existence by essence is prior and more fitting than by attributes, insofar as the essence is self-subsisting and the attributes subsist in it, so that the described is, in terms of essence, prior to the attribute, and the attribute, in terms of necessitation and requirement, is prior to the described. And this is the more repugnant of the two matters. And guidance is sought from God, Most High and Exalted, and He is the best of those who choose.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a rational argument distinguishing between condition and conditioned, using the example of knowledge and power in a living being. The author begins by stating that the distinction is not merely verbal but a rational judgment. He then explains that the difference is exemplified by the relationship between capability (istita'ah) and the act: although both coexist in existence, capability is prior in essence and deservingness, so we say the act occurs by capability, not vice versa. This is the distinction between condition and conditioned: the locus must be alive before knowledge and power can subsist in it; otherwise the distinction collapses. The author warns against reducing this to a verbal or imaginary distinction, arguing that the rational judgment cannot be replaced. He then presents two possible consequences: (1) to abandon the language of causality and conditionality entirely, reducing all to mere linguistic derivation, which is easier; or (2) to affirm a priority of knowledge over being known, and then essence over attributes, leading to a problematic sequence. The author leaves the choice to God, seeking guidance.

مشروط

استطاعة

إيجاب
اقتضاء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The distinction between condition and conditioned is merely a matter of language or imagination, not a real rational priority.
Correction: The author argues that the distinction is a rational judgment (qadiyya 'aqliyya) that cannot be reduced to mere wording or imaginary estimation, as imaginary estimations can be replaced but this judgment cannot.

Checkpoint

B) Capability is prior to the act in existence but not in essence.
C) Capability is prior to the act in essence and deservingness.
D) The act is prior to capability in every respect.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between condition and conditioned is a rational reality, and denying it forces one into either linguistic reductionism or a problematic theological priority.

The Dilemma of Temporal Knowledge and Infinite Regress
Presents a dilemma against temporal knowledge leading to infinite regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a dilemma to refute the Karramiyya's claim that God's knowledge is temporal, showing that it leads to an infinite regress unless grounded in eternal knowledge.

Core Idea
Temporal knowledge without eternal knowledge leads to an infinite regress.

This passage presents a powerful logical dilemma against those who assert that God's knowledge is temporal. The author forces his opponents to answer whether these temporal knowledges are known before they exist. Whichever way they answer, they find themselves cornered: either they must admit eternal knowledge or face an impossible infinite chain. This dilemma effectively refutes the Karramiyya sect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and its translation once to grasp the overall argument. Notice the rhetorical force of the dilemma.
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
The author asks: are temporal knowledges known before they exist? If yes, then either by eternal knowledge or by other temporal knowledges. Mark these two options clearly.
The first branch implies eternal knowledge suffices; the second leads to infinite regress.
3
Trace the consequences of each branch
For the first branch, the author says our answer about created things is your answer – meaning there is no need for temporal knowledge. For the second, he shows it leads to an infinite chain of knowledges.
Infinite regress (tasalsul) is impossible in this context.
4
Note the application to volitions
The author mentions that the same dilemma was applied to proponents of created volitions, but their excuse ('volition is not willed') fails because knowledge is known, so regress is unavoidable for them too.
5
Reflect on the refutation of the Karramiyya
Understand how this dilemma crushes the Karramiyya's position on the substrate of accidents. The lesson is that any temporal attribute requires an eternal foundation to avoid regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ألزم عليهم إلزام لا محيص لهم عنه؛ وهو أن هذه العلوم المتجددة هل معلومة قبل كونها موجودة أم ليس يتعلق العلم بها؟ فإن كانت معلومة أفبعلم الأزلي وعالميته أم بعلوم أخر سبقتها قبل وجودها؟ فإن كان الأول فجوابنا عن الكائنات في كونها معلومة بعلم الأزل جوابكم عن العلوم المتجددة، وإن كان الثاني فكانت محتاجة إلى علوم أخر، والكلام في تلك العلوم كالكلام في هذه، ويؤدي إلى التسلسل، وقد ألزم أصحاب الإرادات الحادثة لا في محل هذا الإلزام واعتذروا بأن قالوا: الإرادة لا تراد وهذا العذر لا يصح على قاعدة هشام وجهم، فإن الإرادة وإن كانت لا تراد فهي بخلاف العلم؛ لأن العلم يعلم فيلزم القول بالتسلسل، وهذا الإلزام قد أفحم الكرامية في مسألة محل الحوادث.
Then he imposed upon them an unavoidable dilemma: are these occurring knowledges known before they come into existence or not? If they are known, then by eternal knowledge and His omniscience, or by other knowledges that preceded them before their existence? If the first, then our answer about created things being known by eternal knowledge is your answer about occurring knowledges. If the second, then they are in need of other knowledges, and the discussion about those knowledges is like the discussion about these, and it leads to infinite regress. And he imposed this dilemma upon the proponents of created volitions not in a substrate, and they excused themselves by saying: volition is not willed. But this excuse is not valid on the basis of Hisham and Jahm, for volition, even if it is not willed, is different from knowledge, because knowledge is known, so the statement of infinite regress is necessary. And this dilemma crushed the Karramiyya on the issue of the substrate of accidents.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a refutation using a dilemma. The author forces an unavoidable dilemma on those who hold that God's knowledge temporally occurs. He asks: Are these temporal knowledges themselves known before they exist? If they are known, then either they are known by God's eternal knowledge or by other temporal knowledges that preceded them. If by eternal knowledge, then the same answer applies to all created things known by eternal knowledge, negating the need for temporal knowledge. If by other temporal knowledges, then those need to be known as well, leading to an infinite regress. The author notes that this dilemma was also applied to those who posit created volitions, and their excuse that 'volition is not willed' fails because knowledge is known, so regress is unavoidable. He concludes that this refutes the Karramiyya on the question of the substrate of accidents.

إرادات حادثة

كرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporal knowledges can be known without any prior knowledge.
Correction: The author demonstrates that temporal knowledges themselves must be known; if not by eternal knowledge, then by other temporal knowledges, which leads to an impossible infinite regress.

Checkpoint

A) They are either eternal or non-existent.
B) They are either known by eternal knowledge or by other temporal knowledges, leading to regress.
C) They are either willed or unwilled.
D) They either change or do not change.

Key Takeaway
The argument shows that positing temporal knowledge in God leads to an infinite regress, thus necessitating eternal knowledge.

God's Knowledge of Universals and Particulars
Shahrastani's exposition of the philosophers' doctrine on God's knowledge of particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' nuanced position that God knows particulars in a universal manner, and how this differs from both absolute particular knowledge and absolute universal knowledge.

Core Idea
God knows particulars universally — not as isolated individuals but as instances of universal causes.

This passage from Shahrastani's work presents the philosophers' intricate view on divine knowledge. It clarifies a common misconception that they deny God's knowledge of particulars. Instead, they affirm a distinct mode of knowledge: God knows particulars in a universal way, subsuming them under causes and universal principles. Let's examine their argument and the three interpretations offered.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the dense philosophical style and the key claim about God's knowledge.
Pay attention to the phrase 'insofar as they are universal'.
2
Understand the eclipse analogy
The analogy illustrates that God's knowledge of universal conditions remains constant before, during, and after the event. This shows His knowledge does not change with particular occurrences.
The analogy is central to explaining how God knows particulars without change.
3
Identify the three philosophical opinions
The passage ends with three positions: some say God knows only His essence, some only universals, some both. See how they are reconciled as different expressions of the same fundamental view.
The author harmonizes them by interpreting each in light of the universal mode of knowledge.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this view contrasts with other theological positions (e.g., Ash'ari occasionalism). Why might philosophers argue that God's knowledge is unchanging?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوجد منها الأمور الجزئية، فالأول يعلم الأسباب ومطابقاتها. فيعلم ما يتأدى إليه، وما بينها من الأزمنة، وما لها من العودات، فيكون مدركًا للأمور الجزئية من حيث هي كلية؛ أعني من حيث لها صفات أو أحوال تستعد بها؛ لأن تكون كلية، وإن تخصصت فبالإضافة إلى زمان ومكان وحال متشخصة. فيعقل ذاته، ونظام الخير والموجود في الكل منه، ونفس مدركة الكل سبب لوجود الكل منه ومبدأ له وإبداع وإيجاب وإيجاد، فلا يتبع علمه معلومًا، بل يسبقه، ولا يكتسب عنه صفة، بل يكسبه، ولا يتغير بتغير المعلوم، بل يغيره، ولا يتعلق بأمر معين من حيث هو معين شخصي، حتى لو زال الشخص زال العلم بوجوده. وهذا كمن عرف أن القمر إذا اجتمع مع الذنب في برج كذا وكانت الشمس في برج مقابل وقع ثم الكسوف، فعلمه هذا قبل وجود الكسوف وبعده وفي حال الكسوف على وجه ليس يتغير بحدوث الكسوف. وكذا كل علم كلي، ثم الجزئي مندرج تحت الكلي على سبيل التضمن، فيصير الكل معلومًا له على هذا الطريق. فمن قال منهم: إنه لا يعقل إلا ذاته، أراد به أنه يعقل كونه مبدأ، وعقله ذلك هو الموجب لحصول ما يصدر عنه. ومن قال منهم: إنه يعقل الكليات دون الجزئيات، أراد ما قررناه. ومن قال منهم: إنه يعقل الكليات والجزئيات، أراد ما عقله مقصودًا؛ أي مبدأ وما يصدر عنه إلا أن ذلك على وجه. فهذا كلام القوم.
There exist among them the particular things. So the First knows the causes and their correspondences. He knows what results from them, the times between them, and their recurrences, so He apprehends particular things insofar as they are universal; that is, insofar as they have attributes or states by which they are disposed to be universal, even if they are specified by relation to a time, place, and individuating circumstance. He intellects His essence, the system of good and existence in the whole from Him, and a soul that apprehends the whole is a cause for the existence of the whole from Him, a principle for it, origination, necessitation, and bringing into existence. So His knowledge does not follow the known, rather it precedes it; it does not acquire a description from it, rather it bestows it; it does not change with the change of the known, rather it changes it; it does not pertain to a particular matter insofar as it is particular and individual, such that if the individual ceased, knowledge of its existence would cease. This is like one who knows that when the moon conjoins with the dragon's head in such a constellation and the sun is in the opposite constellation, an eclipse occurs—his knowledge of this before the eclipse, after it, and during the eclipse is in a manner that does not change with the occurrence of the eclipse. Similarly, every universal knowledge, then the particular is subsumed under the universal by way of inclusion, so the whole becomes known to Him in this way. Among them, whoever said that He intellects only His essence meant that He intellects His being a principle, and His intellection of that necessitates what proceeds from Him. Whoever said that He intellects universals without particulars meant what we have established. Whoever said that He intellects universals and particulars meant what He intellects is intended; that is, the principle and what proceeds from it, but in a certain manner. This is the discourse of the philosophers.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the philosophers' doctrine on divine knowledge. The author (likely Shahrastani) presents their view that God knows particulars in a universal way. He begins by stating that God knows causes and their correspondences, and thus knows the outcomes, times, and recurrences. This knowledge is of particulars insofar as they are universal—that is, through their attributes or states that allow them to be universal, even when specified by time, place, and circumstance. God intellects His essence and the system of good, and His knowledge is active: it precedes the known, does not acquire from it, and does not change with changes in the known. The author gives an analogy: one who knows the conditions for an eclipse has knowledge that remains the same before, during, and after the eclipse. Then he summarizes three opinions among the philosophers: (1) some say God knows only His essence, meaning He knows Himself as the principle; (2) some say He knows universals but not particulars; (3) some say He knows both universals and particulars, but the particulars are known in a universal manner as proceeding from the principle. The passage ends by noting that this is their discourse.

Correction: They affirm knowledge of particulars but in a universal manner, not as isolated individuals.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers affirm that God knows all things, but particulars are known in a universal way, subsumed under causes, and His knowledge is unchanging and creative.

Why the First Intellect's Knowledge of God Causes Multiplicity
Argument that First Intellect's knowledge of God is additional, causing multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the First Intellect's knowledge of God is additional to its essence and leads to multiplicity, in contrast to God's self-knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge of a cause is not a necessary concomitant of self-knowledge.

The author now examines the First Intellect's knowledge of God. He argues that because God is the cause and prior to the First Intellect, knowing God is not automatically entailed by knowing itself. This additional knowledge introduces multiplicity into the First Intellect's essence, a key point in Kalam discussions about divine simplicity and causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its logical flow. Then read the translation to confirm your understanding.
Pay attention to the key terms: 'العقل الأول' (First Intellect) and 'لازم' (necessary concomitant).
2
Identify the main claim
The passage makes a clear claim: knowledge of God is not a necessary concomitant of self-knowledge for the First Intellect. Find the sentence that states this.
Look for the negation 'لا يكون لازما'.
3
Understand the reasoning
The author gives a reason: God is prior to the First Intellect's essence, so knowledge of what is prior cannot follow from knowledge of self. Trace the logical steps: 'مبدأه قبل ذاته' leads to 'لا لازما عنه'.
Contrast this with the case of God's own self-knowledge, where no such priority exists.
4
Note the conclusion
The conclusion is that this knowledge is additional and causes multiplicity. Identify the final phrase: 'فتحصل فيه كثرة بسبب هذا العلم'.
This supports the broader Kalam position that only God is absolutely simple.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما علم العقل الأول بالباري تعالى فلا يكون لازما لعلمه بذاته، فإن مبدأه قبل ذاته لا لازما عنه، فلا يكون العلم بما هو قبل ذاته لازما من نفس علمه بذاته، فيكون علمًا آخر على حدة، والمعلوم ليس نفس العالم ولا لازما منه، فيكون زائدا لا محالة على نفس العالم، فتحصل فيه كثرة بسبب هذا العلم.
As for the knowledge of the First Intellect of the Exalted Creator, it is not a necessary concomitant of its knowledge of its own essence. For its principle is prior to its essence, not a necessary concomitant of it. So knowledge of that which is prior to its essence is not necessary from its own knowledge of its essence; thus it is another knowledge separately. The known is neither the knower itself nor a necessary concomitant of it, so it is necessarily additional to the knower itself, and multiplicity occurs in it due to this knowledge.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage contrasts the case of the First Intellect with that of God. For the First Intellect, its knowledge of God (al-Bārī) is not a necessary concomitant of its self-knowledge because God is the cause prior to its essence. Therefore, this knowledge is additional and separate, causing multiplicity in the First Intellect. The text emphasizes that the object known (God) is neither the knower itself nor a necessary concomitant, so the knowledge is additional.

كثرة

Correction: The author shows that because God is prior, this knowledge is not concomitant but additional, causing multiplicity.

Knowledge, Causality, and the First Intellect
Refutation of a claim about First Intellect's knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the claim that the First Intellect's knowledge of the First Principle is not a necessary consequence of its self-knowledge, and the root error in that argument.

Core Idea
Knowledge of dependence does not necessitate knowledge of the needed one.

This passage presents an opponent's argument about the first effect's knowledge of the First. The opponent claims that such knowledge is separate, not entailed by self-knowledge. The author counters with a logical refutation, exposing a deeper misunderstanding about causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the opponent's claim
Read the Arabic and translation to see what the opponent asserts about the First Intellect's knowledge.
Notice the opponent's reasoning: 'because the beginning of the first effect is before its essence'.
2
Understand the author's refutation
The author rejects the analogy between needing something and knowing it. He states that knowledge can entail other knowledge without requiring a simple entailment.
3
Identify the root error
The author pinpoints that the opponent believes the first effect's existence necessarily follows from the knowledge of the cause, which is a mistake.
This is the key insight: causality does not work that way.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument impacts the understanding of divine knowledge and causality in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : إن علم المعلول الأول بالأول ولا يكون لازما لعلمه بذاته ؛ لأن ومن مبدأ المعلول الأول قبل ذاته فلا يكون العلم بما هو قبل ذاته لازما لعلمه بذاته، فيكون علمًا آخر على حدة يتوجه على مساق كلامه أن كل من علم شيئًا من حاجته وافتقاره لا يلزمه العلم بالمحتاج إليه ؛ لأن المحتاج إليه قبل ذاته وقبل حاجة ذاته ، بل إنما يعلمه بعلم آخر، وليس الأمر كذلك، بل العلم ربما يلزم من العلم، ولا يستدعي لزوم العلم من العلم، أو بالعلم لزوم المعلول من المعلوم، وإنما وقع له هذا الغلط من غلط آخر وهو أنه اعتقد أن المعلول الأول لزم وجوده من علم العلة.
He said: The knowledge of the first effect of the First is not necessary for His knowledge of His essence, because the beginning of the first effect is before its essence, so knowledge of what is before its essence is not a necessary consequence of knowledge of its essence. Thus it is a separate knowledge directed [to that effect]. According to the thread of his speech, whoever knows something of his need and dependence, the knowledge of the needed one is not necessitated for him, because the needed one is prior to his essence and prior to the need of his essence. Rather, he only knows it by another knowledge. But the matter is not so. Rather, knowledge sometimes entails knowledge, and it does not require the necessity of knowledge from knowledge, or the necessity of the effect from the known by knowledge. This error only occurred to him from another error, which is that he believed that the first effect's existence followed from the knowledge of the cause.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents an opponent's argument and the author's refutation concerning the First Intellect (the first effect in Neoplatonic cosmology). The opponent claims that the First Intellect's knowledge of the First Principle is not a necessary consequence of its knowledge of its own essence, because the principle is prior to the essence of the intellect. Therefore, the intellect must have a separate knowledge of the principle. The author rejects this analogy: knowing one's own need does not necessitate knowledge of the needed one, because the needed one is prior. However, the author counters that knowledge can indeed entail other knowledge; there is no requirement that the entailment be from knowledge to knowledge in a simple way. He then diagnoses the root error: the opponent mistakenly believes that the existence of the first effect necessarily follows from the knowledge of the cause. The author implies that this is not a valid inference.

Key Terms
معلول أول
علة
علم
لازم
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The First Intellect's knowledge of the First must be separate and not entailed by its self-knowledge.
Correction: The author argues that knowledge can entail other knowledge; the opponent's reasoning is based on a false assumption about causality.

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the root error in the opponent's argument?
A) That the First Intellect knows itself.
B) That the existence of the first effect follows from the knowledge of the cause.
C) That knowledge cannot be multiple.
D) That the principle is prior.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the idea that knowledge of dependence does not entail knowledge of the needed one, and identifies a mistaken view of causality as the root error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes assume that because you feel dependent on Allah, you already know Him deeply? What specific step will you take today to move from feeling your need to actually seeking knowledge of His attributes?
?
The author warns against thinking that existence follows logically from knowledge. In your own life, have you ever believed that simply knowing a truth should automatically make it real in your actions? What is one truth you know but have not yet lived, and what will you do about it?
?
If knowing something can lead to further knowledge, what is one thing you know about Allah that you can use as a starting point to arrive at a truth you currently do not understand? Commit to exploring that link this week.

Refuting Separate Knowledge in the First Intellect
Refutation of two separate knowledges in the first intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that the first intellect has two distinct knowledges is inconsistent with the principle 'from one only one proceeds.'

Core Idea
Knowledge can entail knowledge without multiplying distinct entities.

The passage confronts a philosophical claim that the first effect (first intellect) must have two separate knowledges: one of itself and one of its cause. The author shows this leads to a contradiction with the established principle that from the one only one proceeds. By following the argument closely, you will see how a correct understanding of causal entailment preserves divine simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
Identify the opponent's argument that knowledge of the cause is separate from self-knowledge because the cause is prior.
This sets up the error that will be refuted.
2
Grasp the author's refutation
Notice how the author rejects the necessity of separate knowledge: knowledge can entail other knowledge without being separate.
The key move is that entailment does not require multiplicity.
3
Trace the opponent's deeper error
The author traces the mistake to a faulty causal chain: God's self-knowledge entails knowledge of the effect, which then entails existence.
The opponent wrongly treats entailment as producing separate acts.
4
See the contradiction with 'from one only one proceeds'
The opponent's view posits two distinct meanings in the first effect, violating the principle that from the one only one proceeds.
This principle is a cornerstone of the opponent's own system.
5
Consider the active intellect analogy
The author argues by analogy: the active intellect has many objects of knowledge without multiplying its essence, so the same applies a fortiori to God.
This reinforces the unity of knowledge in the First.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : إن علم المعلول الأول بالأول لا يكون لازما لعلمه بذاته ؛ لأن مبدأ المعلول الأول قبل ذاته فلا يكون العلم بما هو قبل ذاته لازما لعلمه بذاته، فيكون علمًا آخر على حدة. يتوجه على مساق كلامه أن كل من علم شيئًا من حاجته وافتقاره لا يلزمه العلم بالمحتاج إليه؛ لأن المحتاج إليه قبل ذاته وقبل حاجة ذاته، بل إنما يعلمه بعلم آخر. وليس الأمر كذلك، بل العلم ربما يلزم من العلم، ولا يستدعي لزوم العلم من العلم، أو بالعلم لزوم المعلول من المعلوم. وإنما وقع له هذا الغلط من غلط آخر وهو أنه اعتقد أن المعلول الأول لزم وجوده من علم العلة الأولى به وعلمه به لزوم من علم العلة الأولى بذاتها فظن أن كل شيء علم، وأنه بذاته لزم من علمه بذاته علم بغيره، ولزم من علمه بغيره وجوده، وهذا مجال. على أنه أثبت للمعلول الأول علمين: علم بذاته وعلم آخر بعلته، فيكون قد صدر العلة الأولى شيء هو جوهر قائم بنفسه، قام به معنيان مختلفان محققان، ليس يلزم من أحدهما الثاني، فقد خالف بذلك أصله الممهد أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد. ولا يغنيه عذره بأن أحد الشيئين له لذاته والثاني من علته، فإن علم المعلول بذاته غير وعلمه بالعلة غير، وليس أحد العلمين له لذاته والثاني من علته. ثم هو يعلم ذاته ويعلم معلوله الثاني فقد تكثرت الذات بعلوم زائدة على الذات. على أنه عقل فعال، وإنما عقليته لأنه صورة مجردة عن المادة، وإنما فعاليته لأنه صورة واهبة للصور، فلم تتكثر ذاته بتكثر معلوماته من الصور، ولا تتغير بتغير لوازمه من الموجودات، فهلا كان الأمر في واجب الوجود كذلك.
He said: 'The knowledge of the first effect of the First is not necessary from its knowledge of itself, because the cause of the first effect is prior to itself, so knowledge of what is prior to itself is not necessary from its knowledge of itself; thus it is a separate knowledge.' This follows the implication of his statement that whoever knows something of his need and dependency does not necessarily know that upon which he depends, because that upon which he depends is prior to himself and prior to the need of his self; rather he knows it by another knowledge. But it is not so; rather, knowledge may be entailed by knowledge, and it does not require the entailment of knowledge from knowledge, or by knowledge the entailment of effect from cause. This error occurred from another error: he believed that the existence of the first effect was necessary from the First's knowledge of it, and His knowledge of it was necessary from the First's knowledge of Himself. So he thought that everything is known, and that from His knowledge of Himself there necessarily follows knowledge of another, and from His knowledge of another there follows its existence. This is arbitrary. Moreover, he affirmed for the first effect two knowledges: knowledge of itself and another knowledge of its cause. Then from the first cause issued something that is a self-subsistent substance, in which subsist two distinct and realized meanings, one not entailing the other. Thus he contradicted his established principle that from the one only one proceeds. And his excuse that one of the two things is for itself and the second from its cause does not avail him. For the knowledge of the effect of itself is different from its knowledge of its cause, and one of the two knowledges is not for itself and the second from its cause. Then it knows itself and knows its second effect; so the essence becomes multiplied by extraneous knowledges. Yet it is an active intellect; its intellectuality is because it is a form abstracted from matter, and its activity is because it is a form that bestows forms. So its essence does not become multiplied by the multiplicity of its known forms, nor does it change with the change of its necessary concomitants from existents. Then should not the matter be the same for the Necessary Existence?
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent (likely a philosopher) who claims that the first intellect (the first effect) has two separate knowledges: one of itself and another of its cause (the First). The author begins by quoting the opponent's argument: the first intellect's knowledge of the First is not entailed by its knowledge of itself because the cause is prior; thus it requires a separate knowledge. The author then points out that the opponent's reasoning is flawed because knowledge can entail other knowledge without being separate. The error stems from the opponent's belief that the first intellect's existence follows from God's knowledge of it, which itself follows from God's knowledge of Himself. The opponent's view leads to attributing two distinct meanings (knowledges) to the first intellect, which contradicts the principle 'from one only one proceeds'—a principle the opponent himself holds. The author further argues that even if the first intellect were an active intellect, its knowledge does not multiply its essence, so the same should apply to God a fortiori.

Key Terms
المعلول الأول
العلم
العلة الأولى
الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that God's knowledge of Himself and of creation are separate acts or distinct knowledges.
Correction: God's knowledge is one; knowledge can entail other knowledge without being separate, and multiplicity of known objects does not multiply the essence of the knower.

Checkpoint
What principle does the author say the opponent violates by affirming two distinct knowledges in the first intellect?
A) The principle of non-contradiction
B) The principle that from one only one proceeds
C) The principle that knowledge entails its object
D) The principle that the cause is prior to the effect

Key Takeaway
The claim that the first intellect has two separate knowledges is inconsistent with the principle of unity and with the nature of true knowledge, which can entail without multiplying entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Claim That Will Is Not Willed
Refutes 'will is not willed' with proof and regress avoidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that 'will is not willed' is false and how the chain of wills avoids infinite regress by terminating in God's eternal will.

Core Idea
Every temporal will is willed by God, stopping infinite regress at His eternal will.

The author tackles a key objection: if temporal wills are themselves willed, does that lead to an infinite chain? He argues that the claim 'will is not willed' is baseless because temporal things require a specifying will. He then shows how the regress is avoided by grounding all wills in God's eternal, uncreated will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the author's argument.
Notice the concise style of kalam reasoning.
2
Identify the objection and response
The passage starts by rejecting the claim 'will is not willed'. Find the reasons given for its falsehood.
3
Follow the proof structure
The proof has two parts: 1) temporal things need a will to specify them, 2) if one temporal thing dispensed with will, all would. Understand how this shows temporal wills are willed.
4
Understand the regress solution
The author explains that infinite regress is avoided because the last temporal will is directly created by God's eternal will, which is not specified by time.
This is the key to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قولكم: الإرادة لا تراد تحكم باطل؛ فإن الإرادة من الحوادث، وكل حادث اختص بمثل دون مثل فهو مراد، وإن استغنى حادث عن الإرادة استغنى كل حادث ولا يؤدي إلى التسلسل في الإرادة، فإن الإرادة الأخيرة تستند إلى خلق الباري، فهي ضرورية الوجود مرادة بإرادة الباري تعالى وإرادته قديمة لا تراد؛ أي لا تتخصص بوقت دون وقت.
Then your saying: Will is not willed, is a false judgment; for will is among temporal things, and every temporal thing that is specified with something rather than something else is willed, and if a temporal thing dispensed with will, then every temporal thing would dispense with it, and it does not lead to infinite regress in will, for the last will is based on the creation of the Creator, so it is necessary of existence, willed by the will of God Most High, and His will is eternal, not willed, i.e., it is not specified with a time without a time.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a direct refutation of the claim that 'will is not willed'. The author argues that will is a temporal (contingent) entity. Every temporal entity that is specified with one alternative rather than another requires a will (to specify it). Therefore, temporal wills themselves are willed. If one were to say that a temporal entity can dispense with a will, then all temporal entities would dispense with will, which is absurd. The infinite regress is avoided because the chain of wills ends with God's will, which is eternal and not willed—meaning it is not subject to temporal specification. Thus, temporal wills are created by God and are willed by His eternal will, which itself is not willed.

Key Terms
إرادة
حادث
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if temporal wills are willed, that leads to an infinite regress of wills.
Correction: The author shows that the regress stops at God's eternal will, which is not willed, because it is not a temporal entity requiring specification.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the claim 'will is not willed' is false?
A) Because all temporal things are willed by necessity.
B) Because temporal things need a will to specify them.
C) Because will is eternal.
D) Because will is not temporal.

Key Takeaway
Temporal wills are willed by God's eternal will, which is not itself willed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every temporal will—including your own—is ultimately willed by God, does that frighten you or bring you peace? How does it affect the way you approach the choices you make today?

The author says God's will is eternal and not specified with time. Does your prayer life reflect a belief in a God whose will is outside time, or do you sometimes act as if He is bound by your schedule? What would change if you truly internalized His timelessness?
?
If the alternative to divine will for every temporal thing is chaos (everything would dispense with will), do you live as though your daily actions are random or as though they are encompassed by God's will? What small step can you take today to align your will with His?

Philosophers on Good, Evil, and Providence
Philosophers' classification of good and evil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' view that good enters divine decree essentially while evil enters only accidentally, and you will be able to list the three types of evil they identify.

Core Idea
Good is essential to God's decree; evil is only accidental.

This passage presents the view of the philosophers (falāsifa) on the origin of good and evil. They argue that the entire cosmos is fundamentally good because it emanates from the First Cause, the source of all good. Evil, they claim, enters the world only accidentally and is of three distinct types. Understanding this framework is crucial for appreciating the Islamic theological critique of the philosophers' account of divine providence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the philosophers' key claims about good and evil.
2
Identify the definition of good
Find how the philosophers define 'good' and how it relates to the First Cause. Note the phrase 'yearns for' and 'distinguished'.
Good is what everything desires and perfects existence.
3
Understand how evil is classified
The passage lists three types of evil: deficiency (ignorance, impotence, deformity), pain (suffering, disease), and injustice. See each one.
This classification helps explain why evil is accidental, not essential.
4
Connect to divine providence
The passage calls the emanation 'eternal providence' and 'everlasting will'. Reflect on how this fits with the idea that good is essential and evil accidental.
This sets up the kalām counter-argument about God's omnipotence and justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : النظام في الوجود أولا في العالم متوجه إلى الخير؛ لأنه صادر عن أصل الخير والخير ما يتشوق كل شيء إليه ويتميز به وجود كل شيء، والأول لما علم نظام الخير على الوجه الأبلغ في الإمكان فاض منه ما عقله نظاماً وخيرًا على الوجه الأبلغ فيضاً تاما على أتم تأدية، وذلك هو العناية الأزلية والإرادة السرمدية، فكان الخير داخلا في القضاء الإلهي دخولا بالذات لا بالعرض ثم الشر على وجوه فيقال : شر لمثل النقص الذي هو الجهل والعجز والتشويه في الخلقة، ويقال : شر لمثل الوجع والألم والمرض، ويقال : شر لمثل الظلم
The philosophers said: The system of existence first in the world is directed toward good, because it emanates from the source of good. Good is that toward which everything yearns and by which the existence of everything is distinguished. When the First knew the system of good in the most perfect manner possible, there emanated from Him what He intellectuated as a system of good in the most perfect manner, a complete emanation in the most perfect way. That is the eternal providence and the everlasting will. Thus good entered the divine decree essentially, not accidentally. Then evil is of various kinds: one says 'evil' for such as deficiency—ignorance, impotence, and disfigurement in creation; one says 'evil' for such as pain, suffering, and disease; one says 'evil' for such as injustice.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the philosophers (falāsifa) on the origin of good and evil in the world. They hold that the entire system of existence is fundamentally oriented toward good because it emanates from the First Cause (al-Awwal), which is the source of all good. Good is defined as that which everything desires and by which everything's existence is perfected. When the First Cause intellectuates the best possible system, a perfect emanation flows forth, which is identified as eternal providence (al-ināya al-azaliyya) and everlasting will (al-irāda al-sarmadiyya). Consequently, good enters the divine decree (qaḍā’) essentially (bi al-dhāt), not accidentally (bi al-ʿaraḍ). Evil, however, enters only accidentally. The author then classifies evil into three types: (1) evil as deficiency, such as ignorance, impotence, and deformity; (2) evil as pain, suffering, and disease; and (3) evil as injustice. This sets up a discussion on how evil relates to divine providence, a key issue in kalām against the philosophers.

Key Terms
فلاسفة
خير
شر
عناية أزلية
قضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that philosophers deny the existence of evil altogether or claim that evil is an illusion.
Correction: The philosophers in this passage acknowledge the reality of evil but argue that it enters the world accidentally, not essentially, preserving the absolute goodness of the First Cause.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, how does good relate to the divine decree?
A) It enters essentially
B) It enters accidentally
C) It does not enter at all
D) It enters only in the afterlife

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that good is essential to God's decree, while evil enters only accidentally, classified into deficiency, pain, and injustice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face personal suffering, do you instinctively question God's goodness? How would your heart respond if you truly believed, as this lesson teaches, that evil is only accidental and good is essential to God's decree?
?
Are you more aware of the good or the evil in your daily life? What concrete step can you take today to shift your focus from the accidental evils to the essential goodness that permeates Allah's creation?
?
This lesson defines good as 'that toward which everything yearns.' What do you actually yearn for most? Is your deepest desire aligned with the good that emanates from God, or are you chasing something that distracts you from the eternal providence?

The Philosophers on Good and Evil
Philosophers argue that the system of existence is oriented to good and evil is non-being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that the cosmos is essentially good and that evil is a relative absence of good.

Core Idea
Evil is not a real entity; it is merely the lack of good.

This passage from the philosophers presents a theodicy based on emanation. They argue that since the universe originates from the ultimate Good, it is fundamentally oriented towards good. Evil, they contend, has no positive reality but is simply a relative lack of perfection. Understanding this view is crucial for engaging with classical Islamic philosophical debates on good and evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the original passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation to get a sense of the argument's flow and terminology.
2
Trace the argument for good
Notice how the philosophers derive the goodness of the system from its source in the First, who knows and emanates the best possible order.
Key terms: العناية الأزلية, الإرادة السرمدية
3
Examine the classification of evil
Identify the three ways evil is spoken of: deficiency, pain, and immoral acts. Then see how they are reduced to non-being.
The passage lists three types: شر لمثل النقص, شر لمثل الوجع, شر لمثل الظلم
4
Understand the conclusion on absolute evil
Follow the reasoning that absolute evil cannot exist because any existence would entail good; thus it only exists in words and thought.
الشر المطلق لا وجود له إلا في اللفظ والذهن
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument addresses the problem of evil and what it implies about the nature of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : النظام في الوجود أولا في العالم متوجه إلى الخير؛ لأنه صادر عن أصل الخير والخير ما يتشوق كل شيء إليه ويتميز به وجود كل شيء، والأول لما علم نظام الخير على الوجه الأبلغ في الإمكان فاض منه ما عقله نظاماً وخيراً على الوجه الأبلغ فيضاً تاما على أتم تأدية، وذلك هو العناية الأزلية والإرادة السرمدية، فكان الخير داخلا في القضاء الإلهي دخولا بالذات لا بالعرض ثم الشر على وجوه فيقال : شر لمثل النقص الذي هو الجهل والعجز والتشويه في الخلقة، ويقال : شر لمثل الوجع والألم والمرض، ويقال : شر لمثل الظلم والزنا والسرقة، وبالجملة الشر بالذات هو العدم، ولا كل عدم بل عدم منقص طباع الشيء من الكمالات الثابتة النوعية والطبيعية، والشر المطلق لا وجود له، وكذلك الشر بالذات ليس بأمر حاصل إلا أن يخبر عن لفظه، ولو كان له حصول لكان شراً مطلقاً عاماً ذاتياً موجوداً، وإذا تحقق له وجود فقد حصلت فيه خيرية من جهة وجوده ؛ إذ الوجود من حيث هو وجود خير فتحقق أن الشر المطلق لا وجود له إلا في اللفظ والذهن والوجود على كماله الأقصى أن يكون بالفعل وليس فيه أمر ما بالقوة أصلا، فلا يلحقه شر.
The philosophers said: The system in existence firstly in the world is directed towards good, because it emanates from the principle of good, and good is that towards which everything yearns and by which the existence of everything is distinguished. And the First, when He knew the system of good in the most complete manner possible, there emanated from Him that which He intellected as a system and good in the most complete manner, a perfect emanation in the most perfect realization. That is the eternal care and the eternal will. So good entered into the divine decree essentially, not accidentally. Then evil is of several kinds: it is called evil for deficiency such as ignorance, incapacity, and ugliness in creation; and it is called evil for pain, suffering, and disease; and it is called evil for injustice, adultery, and theft. In sum, evil in itself is non-being, but not all non-being, rather the non-being that diminishes the nature of a thing from the essential and natural perfections. Absolute evil has no existence. Likewise, evil in itself is not an actual thing unless one reports about its word. If it had actuality, it would be absolute, universal, essential evil existing. But if its existence is realized, then good occurs in it from the aspect of its existence, for existence as such is good. So it is established that absolute evil has no existence except in word and mind. And existence in its utmost perfection is to be actual, having no potentiality at all, so evil does not attach to it.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument about the nature of good and evil. It begins by stating that the cosmic system is oriented towards good because it emanates from the source of good. Good is defined as that which everything desires and by which things are distinguished. The First (God), knowing the best possible system, emanates a perfect system; this is called eternal care and will. Good is thus essentially part of divine decree. Then evil is discussed: it is called evil in various senses (deficiency, pain, immoral actions). But evil in itself is non-being, specifically the lack of perfections. Absolute evil has no existence; if it existed, it would be evil absolutely, but any existence brings some good. So absolute evil exists only in words or mind. Existence at its utmost perfection is purely actual with no potentiality, so evil cannot attach to it.

الشر
العدم
العناية الأزلية
الإرادة السرمدية
بالذات
بالعرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers deny that evil exists at all in the world.
Correction: They deny absolute evil's external reality, but acknowledge relative evil as absence of good.

Checkpoint

A) In external reality
B) In word and mind only
C) In both reality and mind
D) In potentiality

Key Takeaway
The philosophers conclude that absolute evil has no existence outside of language and thought, and that all existence is fundamentally good.

Accidental Evil and Its Origin in Matter
Explains accidental evil's existence and its origin in matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accidental evil exists and how it arises from the limitation of the recipient (matter) rather than from the agent (God).

Core Idea
Accidental evil comes from matter's inability to receive perfection, not from God's failure.

In this passage, the author addresses a crucial theological concern: if God is all-powerful and all-good, why does evil exist? The answer is that accidental evil has a real but limited existence, and it stems not from the divine agent but from the inherent limitations of the material recipient. This lesson unpacks that explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms: الشر بالعرض, المادة, الفاعل, المنفعل.
This passage is dense with metaphysical concepts.
2
Identify the cause of evil
Find the phrase 'لا لأن الفاعل قد حرم، بل لأن المنفعل لم يقبل'. This is the core: the agent is not deprived, the recipient did not accept. Understand how this shifts the blame from God to matter's potentiality.
3
Trace the consequences
Follow how the first form prevents perfection, leading to deformed structure, then to bad morals and an animal soul dominating. Notice the chain: matter's limitation leads to moral and spiritual corruption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشر بالعرض فله وجود ما وإنما يلحق ما في طباعه أمر ما بالقوة وذلك لأجل المادة فيلحقها الأمر يعرض لها في نفسها، وأول وجودها هيئة من الهيئات المانعة لاستعدادها الخاص للكمال الذي توجهت إليه فجعلها أردى مزاجاً وأعصى جوهراً لقبول التخطيط والتشكيل والتقويم، فتشوهت الخلقة وانتقصت البنية، لا لأن الفاعل قد حرم، بل لأن المنفعل لم يقبل، فربما يؤدي ذلك إلى أن يصدر من تلك البنية أخلاق رديئة، وربما تستولي نفس حيوانية على نفس إنسانية فيصدر عن الشخص أفعال قبيحة واعتقادات فاسدة.
As for accidental evil, it has some existence. It only attaches to that which has in its nature something potential, and that is because of matter. So it attaches to it an accident that occurs to it in itself. And its first existence is a form from among the forms that prevent its specific readiness for the perfection towards which it is directed, making it of a worse constitution and more refractory substance for receiving planning, shaping, and straightening. So the creation becomes deformed and the structure deficient, not because the agent has been deprived, but because the recipient did not accept. Perhaps that leads to the emergence from that structure of bad morals, and perhaps an animal soul overpowers a human soul, resulting in the person performing ugly deeds and corrupt beliefs.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage defines accidental evil. It has some existence, unlike absolute evil. It attaches to things that have potentiality, due to matter. The first existence of such a thing is a form that prevents its specific readiness for perfection. This results in a worse constitution and a substance less receptive to proper formation. The deformation is not because the agent (God) failed, but because the recipient (the matter) did not accept. This can lead to bad morals and even an animal soul dominating a human soul, causing evil actions and beliefs. The explanation traces evil back to the recipient's limitation.

الاستعداد
الفاعل
المنفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage blames God for evil.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that evil is not because the agent (God) was deprived, but because the recipient (matter) did not accept perfection.

Checkpoint
What is the cause of accidental evil according to this passage?
A) The agent's deprivation
B) The recipient's failure to accept
C) The absence of divine will
D) The inherent evil of matter

Key Takeaway
Accidental evil arises from matter's inability to fully receive perfection, not from any deficiency in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says an animal soul can overpower the human soul, leading to ugly deeds and corrupt beliefs. Where in your life do you allow your lower desires to dominate, preventing your soul from receiving the perfection God intends for you? What concrete step can you take today to reassert your human soul's readiness?
?
God constantly pours out perfection, but your matter may resist. In what area—worship, character, or knowledge—are you refusing to accept His guidance? What is the cost of that resistance, and will you surrender it now?

Accidental Evil's Origin in Matter's Potentiality
Explains accidental evil as arising from matter's potentiality and deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how accidental evil arises from matter's potentiality and deficiency, not from a deficiency in the creator.

Core Idea
Accidental evil stems from matter's failure to receive perfection.

This passage from al-Ash'ari's Kalam explains the nature of accidental evil. It clarifies that evil is not directly created but arises from the inherent limitations of matter. The author distinguishes between the agent's power and the recipient's capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the role of matter
Notice how the author ties accidental evil to matter having potentiality and a restrictive first form.
This is the key causal factor.
3
Understand the agent vs. recipient distinction
The passage explicitly says the defect is not from the agent but from the recipient not accepting.
This guards against blaming God.
4
Explore the consequences
See how deficient matter leads to evil morals and animal souls overpowering human souls.
This shows the chain of effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشر بالعرض فله وجود ما وإنما يلحق ما في طباعه أمر ما بالقوة وذلك لأجل المادة فيلحقها الأمر يعرض لها في نفسها، وأول وجودها هيئة من الهيئات المانعة لاستعدادها الخاص للكمال الذي توجهت إليه فجعلها أردى مزاجاً وأعصى جوهرًا لقبول التخطيط والتشكيل والتقويم، فتشوهت الخلقة وانتقصت البنية، لا لأن الفاعل قد حرم، بل لأن المنفعل لم يقبل، فربما يؤدي ذلك إلى أن يصدر من تلك البنية أخلاق رديئة، وربما تستولي نفس حيوانية على نفس إنسانية فيصدر عن الشخص أفعال قبيحة واعتقادات فاسدة.

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of accidental evil (al-sharr bi al-‘arad). The author states that accidental evil has some existence; it attaches to things that have potentiality (quwwa) in their nature, specifically due to matter. The first form that matter takes may be restrictive, preventing it from achieving its specific readiness for perfection. This results in a worse temperament and a more disobedient substance. The author emphasizes that this is not because the agent (the creator) has been deprived, but because the recipient (matter) did not accept the intended perfection. From such a deficient constitution, evil morals and corrupt actions and beliefs can arise, as a human soul may be overpowered by an animal soul.

The Mutakallimun's Response on Good and Evil
The mutakallimun explain the real dispute is about human actions and natural evils, not definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the mutakallimun's dispute with philosophers is specifically about moral evil in human actions, not about natural evil or the definition of good.

Core Idea
The real debate is about human responsibility for evil, not natural disasters.

The mutakallimun respond to their opponents by narrowing the disagreement. They accept the opponent's definition of pure good and the world's perfection, but insist the real issue is whether human acts—false beliefs, bad morals, evil deeds—are willed by God or by humans. Natural evils like disasters and pains are not part of this dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the concession
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the mutakallimun first concede that they do not dispute the opponent's definition of pure good or the world's perfection. This sets the stage for the real disagreement.
The concession shows they agree on many matters.
2
Locate the real dispute about human actions
Find the sentence beginning with 'Rather, the dispute between us is concerning the goods that pertain to the actions of servants and their acquisitions.' This is the core of the lesson. List the examples given: corrupt beliefs, false ignorances, base morals, ugly deeds.
The key phrase is 'أفعال العباد وإكسابهم'.
3
Understand the stance on natural evils
Read the part where they discuss natural afflictions and pains. They say they do not consider whether these are goods or evils, and they reject the idea that every evil has a hidden good. Instead, they see harm as relative.
This clarifies that the debate is not about natural evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المتكلمون : لا ننازعكم في اصطلاحكم على أن الخير المحض ما هو، أو أن الخير المحض ممن هو، وإن النظام في العالم متوجه إلى كمال في الوجود، وإنما النزاع بيننا في الخيرات التي تتعلق بأفعال العباد وإكسابهم، مثل: الاعتقادات الفاسدة والجهالات الباطلة والأخلاق الردية والأفعال القبيحة أهي حاصلة بإرادتنا دون إرادة الباري أم هي مرادة له عز وجل؟ وما سوى ذلك من الصور القبيحة والحيوانات المؤذية والآفات السماوية والعاهات الأرضية وما يتبعها من الهموم والغموم والآلام والأوجاع فلا نراع فيها أهي خيرات أم شرور، ولا نقول: هي خيرات ومصالح وتحت كل منها شر، ومع كل بلاء ومحنة لطف، وفي كل فتنة وآفة صلاح، ولكل حيوان يؤذي خاصية، ومودع في كل جسم من الأجسام منفعة ومضرة، وكل مضرة فبالنسبة إلى شيء آخر منفعة.
The mutakallimun said: We do not dispute with you about your convention that pure good is what it is, or that pure good is from whom it is, and that the order of the world is directed toward a perfection in existence. Rather, the dispute between us is concerning the goods that pertain to the actions of servants and their acquisitions, such as corrupt beliefs, false ignorances, base morals, and ugly deeds: are they brought about by our will without the will of God, or are they willed by Him, mighty and majestic? As for other than that—ugly forms, harmful animals, heavenly afflictions, earthly defects, and what follows from them of worries, sorrows, pains, and aches—we do not consider whether they are goods or evils, nor do we say: they are goods and benefits with evil under each, or with every calamity and trial there is a kindness, or in every trial and affliction there is a good, or that every harmful animal has a special property, or that in every body there is benefit and harm, and every harm is relative to something else a benefit.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage records the response of the mutakallimun (theologians) to an opponent's position. They first concede that they do not dispute the opponent's conventional definitions of pure good (al-khayr al-mahd) or that the world's order tends toward perfection. The real point of contention is about goods related to human acts and acquisitions (af'al al-'ibad wa iktisabihim)—such as false beliefs, ignorance, bad morals, and evil deeds. Are these caused by human will alone without God's will, or are they willed by God? For other things like ugly forms, harmful animals, natural disasters, and resulting pains, the mutakallimun say they do not worry about classifying them as good or evil. They do not claim that all such things are pure goods with hidden benefits; rather, they acknowledge that every harm is a benefit in relation to something else. The passage thus clarifies that the debate is specifically about moral evil in human actions, not natural evil.

Key Terms
الخير المحض
أفعال العباد
إكساب
الاعتقادات الفاسدة
جهالات
الأخلاق الردية
الأفعال القبيحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the mutakallimun deny the existence of evil entirely or claim that everything is good.
Correction: The passage shows they accept relative benefit for natural evils and focus the dispute on human moral evil, not denying evil.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun, what is the main dispute with their opponents?
A) Whether pure good exists
B) Whether the world has perfection
C) Whether human actions and acquisitions are willed by God or by humans
D) Whether natural disasters are good or evil

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun focus the debate on moral evil in human actions, leaving natural evils as relative and not part of the dispute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a wrong action, do you tend to attribute it to your own free choice, or do you find yourself blaming circumstances, upbringing, or even God's decree? The mutakallimun force us to ask: is this evil willed by God or solely by you? What does your gut reaction reveal about your true belief? Write down one specific sinful habit you have rationalized as 'not really my fault' and commit to facing it as your own acquisition starting today.
?
The mutakallimun set aside natural evils like illness and pain as not being part of the dispute. But when you experience personal suffering, do you secretly resent God or feel He is punishing you? How would your heart change if you accepted that such afflictions are neither inherently good nor evil but simply part of the created order? Describe one recent trial you faced and write a prayer thanking God for it without labeling it as 'good' or 'bad'.
?
The lesson shows that the real debate is about your actions, not about natural disasters. Are you more concerned about the evil you do or the evil that happens to you? Reflect on your daily worries: do they center on your own moral failures or on external misfortunes? What would it mean for your spiritual life to shift your focus to rectifying your own corrupt beliefs and ugly deeds? Make a concrete plan for tomorrow to catch yourself when you shift blame.
