Happiness Requires Effort and Reason's Prudence
The author argues that acquiring knowledge requires effort and reason may advise caution, so happiness is not automatic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the acquisition of knowledge requires effort and why the rational person should be cautious rather than assuming automatic happiness.

Core Idea
Happiness from knowledge is not automatic; it demands effort and rational caution.

This passage begins by addressing the philosophers' assumption that the soul's happiness follows necessarily from acquiring knowledge. The author argues that in reality, acquiring knowledge involves severe difficulties and ambiguous situations, and human nature alone is insufficient. Therefore, a rational person should stop and think rather than plunge into risk.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial conditional
Notice the 'If... then' structure: happiness depends on acquiring knowledge and action, which requires effort.
2
Identify the practical difficulty
The author highlights that acquiring knowledge involves enduring severe matters and difficult conditions.
3
Consider reason's response
When matters are ambiguous, reason stops to avoid falling into ignorance. This is the basis for the author's conclusion that stopping is better than plunging.
4
Reflect on the outcome
The conclusion is that happiness is not automatically attained; it requires cautious effort.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : إذا كان سعادة النفس مترتبة على حصول قوى العلم والعمل، وخروجهما من القوة إلى الفعل يحتاج إلى معاناة أمور شديدة ومقاساة أحوال عسيرة من تحصيل المقدمات والوقوف على كيفية تأديها بالطلب أو المطلوب، ولم تكن الفطرة الإنسانية بمجردها كافية في التحصيل وتعارض الأمر عند العقل يوقف العاقل لئلا يق في طريق يفضي به إلى الجهالة الموجبة للشقاوة، فإن التوقف أولى من اقتحام الخطر في المهالك. وأيضا فإن عندكم مخرج العقل من القوة إلى الفعل يجب أن يكون عقلا بالفعل، فإن العقل بالقوة لا يخرج العقل بالقوة، فإنه بعد محتاج إلى مخرج، وليس هذا بذاته يخرج ذاته من القوة إلى الفعل، كما أن الممكن بذاته لا يترجح أحد طرفي إمكانه على الثاني إلا بمرجح كذلك ما هو بالقوة فلا يخرج من القوة إلى الفعل إلا بمخرج، وكما أن المرجح على الحقيقة ما انتفى عنه وجوه الإمكان، كذلك المخرج
It is said: If the happiness of the soul is dependent on the acquisition of the powers of knowledge and action, and their coming out from potentiality to actuality requires enduring severe matters and experiencing difficult conditions — acquiring premises and knowing how to perform them by seeking or the sought — and human nature alone is not sufficient for acquisition, and when a matter is ambiguous for reason, it makes the rational person stop so that he does not fall into a path that leads him to ignorance causing misery, then stopping is better than plunging into danger in perils. Moreover, for you, the one who brings the intellect from potentiality to actuality must be an intellect in actuality, for potential intellect does not bring out potential intellect; it is still in need of an external cause. This [intellect] does not by itself bring itself from potentiality to actuality, just as a possible thing does not by itself prefer one of its two possible states over the other except by a preponderating factor (murajjih), so likewise that which is in potentiality does not come out from potentiality to actuality except by an external cause (mukharrij), and just as the true preponderating factor is that from which aspects of possibility are absent, so also the external cause...
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is the author's response to the philosophers' claim that reward and punishment are necessary consequences. The author makes two arguments. First, if happiness were automatically tied to knowledge, it would happen without effort, but in reality, acquiring knowledge requires great effort and difficult circumstances. Since human nature is not sufficient, and reason warns against risking ignorance, the rational person should be cautious. Second, the philosophers themselves hold that a potential intellect requires an actual intellect to bring it to actuality. A potential thing cannot actualize itself; it needs an external cause. Analogous to the principle that a possible thing needs a preponderating factor (murajjih) to determine one state over another, so too potentiality needs an external cause to become actual. Therefore, reward and punishment cannot be automatic consequences; they depend on external factors and effort.

Key Terms
قوة (potentiality)
فعل (actuality)
سعادة (happiness)
شقاوة (misery)
فطرة (nature)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge automatically brings happiness without effort.
Correction: The author argues that acquiring knowledge requires severe effort and reason may advise caution, so happiness is not automatic.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about human nature's role in acquiring knowledge?
A) It is sufficient.
B) It is not sufficient.
C) It is only for the prophets.
D) It is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Happiness from knowledge is not automatic; it demands effort and rational prudence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own journey to gain knowledge, when have you faced an ambiguous matter and chose to stop rather than plunge ahead? What did that cost you, and what did it save you from?
?
Are you willing to endure the severe matters and difficult conditions required to achieve true happiness, or do you expect it without effort? Be honest: where does your current effort fall short?
?
If reason itself sometimes advises caution, why do you often rush into decisions without reflection? What ignorance might you be courting by ignoring that inner stop sign?

Potential Intellect Requires an External Cause
The author argues that, on the philosophers' own principles, a potential intellect requires an actual intellect as an external cause to become actual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a potential intellect cannot actualize itself and needs an external cause, analogous to the principle of preponderating factor in possible things.

Core Idea
A potential intellect cannot actualize itself; it requires an external cause in actuality.

The author now presents a second argument that turns the philosophers' own principles against them. They agree that a potential intellect needs an actual intellect to bring it to actuality, and that a possible thing needs a preponderating factor to incline to one state. The author applies this to the soul's happiness, showing that it cannot be automatic but requires an external cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Notice the shift: 'Moreover, for you, the one who brings the intellect from potentiality to actuality must be an intellect in actuality.'
2
Understand the analogy with possible things
Just as a possible thing cannot prefer one state without a preponderating factor, potentiality cannot become actual without an external cause.
3
Identify the conclusion
Therefore, the soul's actualization of knowledge cannot be automatic; it requires an external cause, supporting the need for effort and guidance.

Source Text

قيل : إذا كان سعادة النفس مترتبة على حصول قوى العلم والعمل، وخروجهما من القوة إلى الفعل يحتاج إلى معاناة أمور شديدة ومقاساة أحوال عسيرة من تحصيل المقدمات والوقوف على كيفية تأديها بالطلب أو المطلوب، ولم تكن الفطرة الإنسانية بمجردها كافية في التحصيل وتعارض الأمر عند العقل يوقف العاقل لئلا يق في طريق يفضي به إلى الجهالة الموجبة للشقاوة، فإن التوقف أولى من اقتحام الخطر في المهالك. وأيضا فإن عندكم مخرج العقل من القوة إلى الفعل يجب أن يكون عقلا بالفعل، فإن العقل بالقوة لا يخرج العقل بالقوة، فإنه بعد محتاج إلى مخرج، وليس هذا بذاته يخرج ذاته من القوة إلى الفعل، كما أن الممكن بذاته لا يترجح أحد طرفي إمكانه على الثاني إلا بمرجح كذلك ما هو بالقوة فلا يخرج من القوة إلى الفعل إلا بمخرج، وكما أن المرجح على الحقيقة ما انتفى عنه وجوه الإمكان، كذلك المخرج
It is said: If the happiness of the soul is dependent on the acquisition of the powers of knowledge and action, and their coming out from potentiality to actuality requires enduring severe matters and experiencing difficult conditions — acquiring premises and knowing how to perform them by seeking or the sought — and human nature alone is not sufficient for acquisition, and when a matter is ambiguous for reason, it makes the rational person stop so that he does not fall into a path that leads him to ignorance causing misery, then stopping is better than plunging into danger in perils. Source pages p. 388

This passage is the author's response to the philosophers' claim that reward and punishment are necessary consequences. The author makes two arguments. First, if happiness were automatically tied to knowledge, it would happen without effort, but in reality, acquiring knowledge requires great effort and difficult circumstances. Since human nature is not sufficient, and reason warns against risking ignorance, the rational person should be cautious. Refutation of Infinite Regress in Actions and Rewards
The author refutes infinite regress and proves a first action and final reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why infinite regress in the sequence of actions and rewards leads to logical circularity and why there must be a first action and a final reward.

Core Idea
Infinite regress of causes and effects leads to circular impossibility; a first cause is necessary.

This passage presents a crisp logical argument against the idea of an infinite chain of actions and rewards. The author demonstrates that such infinite regress collapses into circular reasoning, making the sequence impossible. Understanding this argument is key to grasping the Islamic theological position on the finitude of causal chains.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once without translating. Notice the key terms تسلسل and دور.
2
Understand the circularity argument
The author shows that if every action requires a prior reward and every reward requires a prior action, we have mutual dependence that never begins.
3
Compare to cause-effect
The author likens this to the impossible dependence of effect on cause and cause on effect.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The only way out is to posit a first action not caused by a reward and a final reward not leading to an action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قضيتم بالتسلسل فذلك دور محض، فإنه إن لم يكن فعل إلا جزاء ولا جزاء إلا على فعل فلم يكن فعل وجزاء أصلا، فإنه يتوقف كون الجزاء جزاء على سبق فعل، ويتوقف كون الفعل فعلا على سبق جزاء، فيكون كل واحد منهما متوقفاً على صاحبه فيكون حكمه حكم توقف المعلول على العلة، وتوقف العلة على المعلول وذلك محال، فلا بد من ابتداء بفعل أولي ليس بجزاء، والانتهاء إلى جزاء آخر ليس بفعل، وذلك تسليم المسألة.
For if you posit infinite regress, that is pure circularity, because if there is no action except as a reward and no reward except for an action, then there would be no action and no reward at all. For the reward's being a reward depends on a preceding action, and the action's being an action depends on a preceding reward. Consequently, each of them depends on the other, so its ruling is the ruling of the effect's dependence on the cause and the cause's dependence on the effect, and that is impossible. Thus there must be a beginning with a first action that is not a reward, and an end with a final reward that is not an action, and that is conceding the point.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is a proof against infinite regress (tasalsul) in the context of actions and rewards. It begins by stating that if one posits an infinite chain of actions and rewards, it leads to pure circularity (dawr). The reasoning: an action can only exist as a reward, and a reward can only exist for an action. This mutual dependence means that neither can exist without the other, but if we go back infinitely, we never get a starting point. The author compares this to the impossible situation of the effect depending on the cause and the cause depending on the effect. Therefore, there must be a first action that is not a reward, and a final reward that is not an action. This establishes the necessity of a starting point and an end, which is what the opponent (reincarnationist?) would deny.

Key Terms
تسلسل
دور
جزاء
فعل
علة
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite regress of actions and rewards is possible without a starting point.
Correction: The author shows that infinite regress leads to circular dependence, making the sequence impossible. Thus a first action and final reward are necessary.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the impossibility of infinite regress of actions and rewards?
A) There is no action or reward at all.
B) There must be a first action that is not a reward and a final reward that is not an action.
C) Actions and rewards are independent of each other.
D) The chain can continue infinitely if it is not circular.

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress of actions and rewards is impossible because it creates a circular dependence; therefore there must be a first action and a final reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your good deeds are waiting for a reward that never comes because you keep focusing on what comes next? How does knowing there is a final reward change how you view your actions today?
?
The passage says that without a first action, nothing exists. Does your life reflect a belief in a First Cause who initiated everything, or do you sometimes feel as if you are self-caused and self-sustained?
?
When you fail to do good, do you view it as just another step in an endless cycle of actions and consequences, or do you realize that the cycle is broken by a final judgment with Allah? How can you orient your life toward that final reward starting now?

Impossibility of Two Souls in One Body
The author refutes reincarnation by arguing it would require two souls in one body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that reincarnation would necessitate two souls in one body, which is impossible within the framework of emanation of forms.

Core Idea
Reincarnation fails because one body cannot host two souls.

This passage presents a crisp argument from the tradition of philosophical theology (kalam) against the doctrine of reincarnation. It relies on the metaphysical concept of the Giver of Forms and the idea that each body receives a single form. Understanding this argument helps clarify why many Muslim theologians rejected reincarnation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify key technical terms
Locate the terms tanasukh (reincarnation), mizaj (temperament), sura (form), Wahib al-Suwar (Giver of forms), and nafs (soul). Understanding these is essential.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Walk through the causal chain
Identify the hypothetical scenario: a body's temperament is prepared, form overflows from the Giver, and the 'desirable soul' from a past life accompanies that form. Follow the logic step by step.
The argument is conditional: 'if... then...'.
4
Grasp the impossibility
The conclusion is that one body would end up with two souls (the new form's soul and the old soul). The author asserts this is impossible. Ask yourself: why is it impossible? Reflect on the unity of a living being.
5
Consider the metaphysical assumptions
This argument depends on the theory of emanation of forms. Think about how different metaphysical frameworks might affect the validity of the proof.
The common misunderstanding highlights this dependency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يبطل التناسخ رأسًا إن كان مزاج استعد لقبول الصورة يلائمها من واهب الصور، فإذا فاضت عليه الصورة قارنتها صورة النفس المستحسنة لزم أن يكون لبدن واحد نفسان وذلك محال.
And among things that invalidate reincarnation entirely is that if a temperament is prepared to receive a form suitable to it from the Giver of forms, then when the form overflows upon it, the form of the noble soul would accompany it, then it would be necessary that one body have two souls, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents another argument refuting the doctrine of reincarnation (tanasukh). It is based on the theory of emanation of forms from the Giver of forms (Wahib al-Suwar). The argument: a particular bodily temperament (mizaj) is prepared to receive a specific form from the Giver. When that form is emanated, if it were accompanied by the form of a soul from a previous life (the 'desirable' soul), then the same body would have two souls simultaneously. This is impossible because one body cannot have two souls. Therefore, reincarnation cannot occur.

Key Terms
تناسخ
مزاج
صورة
واهب الصور
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves reincarnation is impossible in all frameworks.
Correction: The argument depends on the theory of emanation of forms from the Giver of Forms; it refutes reincarnation only if one accepts that metaphysical framework.

Checkpoint
What impossibility does the author use to refute reincarnation?
A) The body cannot receive any form.
B) The Giver of forms does not exist.
C) One body would have two souls.
D) The temperament cannot be prepared.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes reincarnation by showing that it would result in two souls for one body, an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself longing for a second chance in a different body, perhaps to escape the consequences of your actions? How does this lesson's insistence on the impossibility of two souls in one body force you to confront the finality of your choices and the accountability you bear before Allah?
?
Consider how easily we cling to worldly attachments as if they will transfer with us to another existence. This argument shows that even if reincarnation were possible, it would be absurd. What attachments have you been nurturing that you need to release, knowing that only the soul's state matters in the Hereafter?
?
The Giver of forms bestows forms according to preparedness. Are you actively preparing your inner temperament (your heart) to receive divine guidance, or are you expecting change without effort? How does this lesson challenge you to take responsibility for your spiritual state here and now, rather than hoping for a future do-over?

The Philosophers' Rejection of Any Cause in God's Acts
Philosophers argue that the Necessary Existent cannot act for any cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the Necessary Existent cannot act for any cause and how this position differs from the Ash'ari view.

Core Idea
The philosophers deny any motive in God's actions, but on metaphysical necessity, not divine will.

This passage presents the view of the philosophers (falāsifa) that the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd) cannot act for any cause or motive. They specifically reject seeking praise, obtaining benefit, or any external call. This position is reported as part of a refutation, and distinguishing it from the Ash'ari stance is essential for understanding the theological debate over divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the precise negations.
The passage is compact; focus on the three excluded motives.
2
Identify the rejected motives
List the three types of causes the philosophers deny: praise/gratitude, benefit/harm, and any summoning matter.
These cover possible purposes one might ascribe to God.
3
Compare with Ash'ari view
Both philosophers and Ash'aris reject purposes in God's acts, but the philosophers base it on the nature of the Necessary Existent, while Ash'aris ground it in divine will and power.
This distinction is key to the author's refutation later.
4
Reflect on the debate's significance
Consider how this view challenges the Mu'tazilite notion of divine purpose and sets the stage for a deeper discussion on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قالت الفلاسفة : واجب الوجود لا يجوز أن يفعل فعلا لعلة، لا ليحمد ويتزين بالحمد والشكر ولا لينتفع أو يدفع الضرر، ولا لأمر داع يدعوه ويحمله
And the philosophers said: The Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd) may not do an act for a cause—neither to be praised and adorned with praise and gratitude, nor to benefit or repel harm, nor for any summoning matter that calls and impels Him.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' view. They assert that the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd) cannot act for any cause ('illa). They specifically negate three possible motives: (1) seeking praise or adornment with praise and gratitude, (2) seeking benefit or repelling harm, and (3) any external call or impulsion. Thus, the philosophers agree with the Ash'arites in denying purpose and cause in God's actions, but their reasoning is based on the nature of the Necessary Existent rather than on the attribute of will. The author reports this view as part of the refutation, likely to show that even the philosophers, despite their differences, reject the Mu'tazilite notion of purpose.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
علة
حمد
شكر
داع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the philosophers' denial of cause is identical to the Ash'ari denial of purpose.
Correction: Although both reject purposes, the philosophers base it on the metaphysical necessity of the Necessary Existent, while Ash'aris base it on God's absolute will and power.

Checkpoint
What do philosophers deny about the Necessary Existent's action?
A) That He acts at all
B) That He acts for any cause
C) That He creates the world
D) That He has attributes

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' denial of all causes in God's actions underscores their commitment to divine transcendence, though from a metaphysical necessity standpoint distinct from the Ash'ari theological foundation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you find yourself thinking that God should have prevented it because it seems to you like a harm? How does this passage's denial that God acts to 'repel harm' challenge your expectation that God's actions must align with your perception of benefit?
?
Do you ever do good deeds for the sake of being praised or thanked? If God, who is completely independent, does not act for praise or gratitude, what does that say about the purity of intention you should seek in your worship?

Necessary Being Acts Without Purpose and by Necessity
Philosophers argue the Necessary Being cannot act for a cause and that the world emanates necessarily.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that the Necessary Being cannot act for a cause and that all existence emanates necessarily from the Active Intellect.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being acts by necessity, not by purpose.

This passage presents the core of the philosophers' (falasifa) view on divine action. They argue that the Necessary Being, being perfect and self-sufficient, cannot act for any cause or motive. Instead, all things emanate from Him necessarily through the Active Intellect. Understanding this view is crucial for contrasting it with the Ash'arite and Mu'tazilite positions you will encounter later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense style and list of motives.
2
Identify the five motives ruled out
The passage lists five specific motives that the Necessary Being does not act for. Find each one and note how they are ruled out.
These include praise, benefit, repelling harm, inner motive, and the high desiring for the low.
3
Understand the emanation model
The passage then explains that actions emanate from principles and the Active Intellect. Focus on the terms 'abda'a' and 'luzūm'.
Emphasize that emanation is necessary and impersonal.
4
Reflect on the contrast with other views
Ask yourself how this view differs from the Ash'arite or Mu'tazilite emphasis on divine will and purpose. This will prepare you for later lessons.
Note that the philosophers deny any volitional attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قالت الفلاسفة : واجب الوجود لا يجوز أن يفعل فعلا لعلة، لا ليحمد ويتزين بالحمد والشكر ولا لينتفع أو يدفع الضرر، ولا لأمر داع يدعوه ويحمله على الفعل، والعالي لا يريد أمرًا لأجل السافل، بل الأفعال صدرت عن المبادئ وهي استندت إلى العقل الفعال، وإنما أبدع العقل بلوازمه، وأبدع بتوسطه سائر الأشياء على وجه اللزوم عنه ضرورة ؛ إذ ليس يتصور وجود واجب الوجود إلا كذلك.
And the philosophers said: The Necessary Being is not allowed to perform an act for a cause, not to be praised and adorned with praise and gratitude, nor to benefit or repel harm, nor for any motive that calls and impels him to the act. The high does not desire something for the sake of the low. Rather, actions emanated from the principles, and they relied on the Active Intellect. The Intellect only originated (abda'a) with its concomitants, and through its intermediation originated all other things necessarily—by the mode of emanation from it by necessity; because the existence of the Necessary Being is not conceivable except thus.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (falasifa) position on divine action. They argue that the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud) cannot act for any cause or purpose. Five specific motives are ruled out: (1) seeking praise or thanks, (2) benefiting Himself, (3) repelling harm from Himself, (4) any internal motive (dā'in), and (5) the high desiring something for the low. Instead, actions proceed from 'principles' (mabādi') that are dependent on the Active Intellect (al-'aql al-fa''āl). The Active Intellect 'originated' (abda'a) its own concomitants, and through it all other things emanated necessarily by what some call the 'mode of emanation' (wajh al-luzūm). The passage concludes that the existence of the Necessary Being is only conceivable in this way—as a necessary, impersonal source of emanation rather than a volitional agent. This contrast with the Ash'arite and Mu'tazilite views highlights the philosophical commitment to a causally determined cosmic order.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
علة
داعي
العقل الفعال
إبداع
لزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is often misunderstood as atheistic or denying God's existence.
Correction: It is actually a theistic emanationist model common in Islamic philosophy; the philosophers affirm the Necessary Being but deny that He acts with purpose.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why does the Necessary Being not act for a cause?
A) Because He is wise and acts only for the best.
B) Because He is perfect and needs nothing, and actions emanate necessarily.
C) Because He is powerless to create directly.
D) Because He is limited by time.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that the Necessary Being's actions are necessary and without purpose, emanating through the Active Intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for a need, do you assume God's action is motivated by a sense of purpose or just a necessary emanation? Does this philosopher's view challenge you to reconsider why you supplicate? Will you examine your own belief about God's will and causality after reading this?
?
If God's actions are necessary and without purpose, what does that mean for your understanding of gratitude and praise? Are you praising a being who cannot be motivated by praise? How does that affect your worship?
?
The philosophers say 'the high does not desire for the low'. After this lesson, will you check your heart: do you worship God expecting Him to benefit you, or do you worship Him purely because He is the Necessary Being? What would change in your prayer if you took this seriously?

Impossibility of Contradictory Indications from Signs
Proof that a sign cannot indicate both truth and falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a miracle cannot indicate both truth and falsehood, and why God cannot perform contradictory signs.

Core Idea
A sign's directive force is fixed; it cannot indicate its opposite.

This passage presents a key proof in Islamic theology: the directive force (jiha al-dalāla) of a sign is immutable. Once a miracle confirms a prophet's truth, it cannot also indicate deception. The author grounds this in logical necessity and divine capacity, emphasizing that such contradiction would be impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the term 'جهة الدلالة' (directive force) and the examples given.
The author uses technical language; read slowly.
2
Understand the analogy with specification
The author compares the directive force of a sign to specification (التخصيص) indicating will. Just as specification cannot indicate its opposite, a sign cannot indicate the opposite of its natural indication.
3
Analyze the impossible scenarios
Identify the three scenarios the author declares impossible: sending a messenger without proof, a miracle intended to mislead, and a miracle at the hands of a liar. Understand why each leads to logical contradiction.
The author concludes such acts are impossible due to contradiction, not moral ugliness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن وجه تناقض الدليل أن جهة الدلالة في القرينة وفي الفعل لا تختلف، فإذا دل الشيء على شيء لا يجوز أن يدل على خلافه، فإن ذلك غير مقدور. كما أن جهة التخصيص إذا دلت على الإرادة لم تدل على خلاف ذلك، فجهة التخصيص بالتصديق لا تدل على خلاف قصد المخصص بالتصديق، فإرسال رسول وإخلاؤه عن دليل الصدق وإظهار معجزة والقصد بها إلى إضلال الخلق وإظهار خارق للعادة على يدي كاذب في معارضة دعوى النبي كل ذلك محال، لما ذكرناه أنه يؤدي إلى محال، كإخلاء النظر الصحيح التام عن الإفضاء إلى العلم، فإنه إذا تم وجب وجود العلم لا محالة، وكنصب دلالة التوحيد لتدل على الشرك والإضلال على الإطلاق يجوز أن يضاف إلى الله تعالى؛ بمعنى أنه يخلق ضلالا في قلب شخص، لكنه إذا أدى إلى محال فهو محال فلا يفعله لتناقضه واستحالته ولا لقبحه وبشاعته.
And from the aspect of the contradiction of the proof: the directive force in the indicator and in the action does not differ. So if a thing indicates something, it is not permissible for it to indicate its opposite, for that is not within power. Just as the aspect of specification, when it indicates will, does not indicate its opposite, so the aspect of specification by affirmation does not indicate the opposite of the intention of the one who specifies by affirmation. Thus, sending a messenger and leaving him without a proof of truthfulness, or manifesting a miracle with the intention of misleading creation, or manifesting a supernatural event at the hands of a liar in opposition to the claim of a prophet—all that is impossible, because it leads to impossibility, like the complete and sound reflection being devoid of leading to knowledge, for when it is complete, knowledge necessarily exists. And like setting up a proof of tawḥīd to indicate shirk. And misguidance in an absolute sense may be attributed to God, meaning that He creates misguidance in a person's heart. But if it leads to impossibility, it is impossible, so He does not do it due to its contradiction and impossibility, not due to its ugliness or repulsiveness.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage is a third proof against the possibility of God using contradictory signs. The author argues that the directive force (jiha al-dalāla) of a sign is fixed: a thing cannot indicate both a proposition and its opposite, as that is beyond power. He gives an analogy: specification (takhṣīṣ) for an act indicates will, not the opposite. So a sign that confirms a prophet's truthfulness cannot also indicate the opposite—that is, deception. Then he lists several scenarios that are all impossible: sending a prophet without proof of truth, showing a miracle intending to mislead, or showing a miracle at the hands of a liar. All are impossible because they lead to contradiction, like a perfect logical proof failing to produce knowledge. He clarifies that occasional misguidance (iḍlāl) can be attributed to God as creating error in a heart, but if it leads to an impossibility like contradicting a promise, it is impossible—not because of evil, but because of logical contradiction.

Key Terms
تناقض الدليل
جهة الدلالة
التخصيص
محال
الإضلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God could show miracles through liars to test faith or for some other purpose.
Correction: The author stresses that this would contradict the miracle's own indication as a proof of truth, leading to logical impossibility. God does not do impossible things, not because they are ugly, but because they are self-contradictory.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is showing a miracle at the hands of a liar impossible?
A) Because it is morally repulsive.
B) Because it would contradict the directive force of the miracle as a proof of truth.
C) Because God lacks the power to do so.
D) Because prophets would be offended.

Key Takeaway
A miracle that confirms truth cannot simultaneously indicate falsehood; such contradiction is impossible for God due to logical necessity, not moral defect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a clear sign from God—whether in the Qur'an, in nature, or in your life—do you treat its indication as fixed, or do you try to twist it to suit your desires? What would it cost you to submit fully to what it demands?
?
This lesson argues that logical contradiction is impossible even for God. Are there areas in your faith where you hold contradictory beliefs—for example, claiming to trust God’s plan while living in constant anxiety? How will you resolve that contradiction today?
?
If a miracle from a false prophet is impossible, then Muhammad’s ﷺ miracle (the Qur’an) demands your absolute commitment. Have you given that miracle its due weight in your daily decisions, or have you reduced it to a mere intellectual affair? What is one step you will take this week to align your actions with the truth it proves?

The Indivisible Atom: Definition and Positions
Defines the issue of the indivisible atom and contrasts the two schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the mutakallimun mean by the indivisible atom and how their view contrasts with that of the philosophers.

Core Idea
The indivisible atom is the necessary endpoint of finite division.

Al-Juwayni begins by defining the central issue: can a body be divided infinitely? The mutakallimun say no, because a finite body cannot contain infinite parts. The philosophers disagree. This lesson clarifies the two positions and their underlying conceptions of body composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening definition
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning to the point where it contrasts the mutakallimun and philosophers. Notice how the issue is framed.
The term 'jawhar fard' is introduced here.
2
Compare the two views
The mutakallimun define the body as composed of finite parts; the philosophers see it as matter and form. Identify the key difference.
3
Reflect on the stakes
Consider why this distinction matters for theology. The existence of atoms supports the possibility of creation and divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في إثبات الجوهر الفرد. الجسم ينتهي بالتجزئة إلى حد لا يقبل الوصف بالتجزي ويسميه المتكلمون جوهرًا فردًا، وصارت الفلاسفة إلى أنه لا ينتهي إلى حد لا يقبل الوصف بالتجزي. ومدار المسألة على أن الجسم عند المتكلم هو المركب من أجزاء متناهية، وما تحصره النهايات والأطراف لا يشتمل على ما لا نهاية له، وعند الفيلسوف الأجزاء إنما تحدث بالفعل في الجسم إما رضًا وكسرًا وإما بانتشاره وإما باختلاف عرضين وإما بالوهم والقوة، والجسم مركب من هيولى وصورة لا من أجزاء متحيزة. ودليل المتكلم في المسألة أن المتناهي أطرافه وأضلاعه يستحيل أن يشتمل على منقسمات بلا نهاية فإن المحصور بالنهايات لا يكون حاصرًا بما لا نهايات له، وأيضًا فإن الاتصال المحسوس في الجسم متناه بالحس والانفصال يستدعي سبق اتصال لا محالة، فلو كان الجسم مما ينفصل عن النهاية فليكن فيه الاتصال لغير النهاية، فإن كان الانفصال بالفعل فيسبقه الاتصال بالفعل وإن كان الانفصال بالقوة والوهم فليكن فيه الاتصال بالقوة والوهم، ثم اتصاله متناء قوة وفعلا.
Issue on proving the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). The body ends by division at a limit that does not accept description by division, and the mutakallimun call it an indivisible atom. The philosophers have taken the position that it does not end at a limit that does not accept description by division. The focus of the issue is that the body according to the mutakallimun is composed of finite parts, and what is bounded by limits and sides does not contain that which has no end. According to the philosopher, the parts only arise in the body in actuality either by breaking and compression, or by its expansion, or by difference of two accidents, or by imagination and potentiality, and the body is composed of matter (hayula) and form (sura), not of quantified parts. The proof of the mutakallimun in the issue is that that whose sides and edges are finite is impossible to contain infinite divisions, for that which is enclosed by limits cannot encompass that which has no limits. Also, the sensed connection (ittisal) in the body is finite by sensation, and separation (infisal) necessarily requires a prior connection. So if the body were such that it separates without end, then let there be in it connection without end. If the separation is actual, then actual connection precedes it; if the separation is potential and hypothetical, then let there be in it potential and hypothetical connection. Then its connection is finite in potentiality and actuality.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage presents the classical kalam proof for the existence of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard). It begins by defining the issue: the body, when divided, reaches a limit that cannot be further divided, called by the mutakallimun an indivisible atom. The philosophers deny this, claiming division can continue infinitely. The key contrast is that for the mutakallimun, a body is composed of finite parts, so that which is limited cannot contain infinite parts. For the philosopher, parts arise only in actuality through various processes, and the body is composed of matter and form, not discrete quantified parts. Then the mutakallimun give their proof: a finite object (limited in sides and edges) cannot contain infinite divisions, because what is enclosed by limits cannot encompass what is limitless. Furthermore, the connection we sense in bodies is perceived as finite, and separation must be preceded by connection. If separation could go on infinitely, then connection would have to be infinite as well, which contradicts sensory experience. Whether separation is actual or potential, the preceding connection must be of the same modality (actual or potential). Therefore, the connection is finite in both potentiality and actuality, implying that division cannot proceed infinitely and must terminate at an indivisible unit.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد
جسم
تجزئة
متكلمون
فلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The indivisible atom is a physical particle like a modern atom.
Correction: It is a logical/metaphysical unit derived from the finitude of division, not necessarily a physical particle.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun, what is a body composed of?
A) Infinite parts
B) Finite parts (atoms)
C) Matter and form
D) A single continuous substance

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun define the body as a finite composite of parts, ending at an indivisible atom, while philosophers deny such an endpoint.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the body is composed of finite parts that end at an indivisible atom, then creation has a definite structure and purpose. How does this affect your daily awareness that every atom of your existence is known and accounted for by Allah? Will you today reflect on the precision of creation and your place in it?
?
The philosophers' view suggests that division could go on forever, implying an endless complexity. Does this make you feel more amazed at the Creator's infinite power, or does it create doubt about His control? Be honest: have you ever felt overwhelmed by the vastness of creation? How does the mutakallimun's clear limit (the atom) bring you back to tawhid?

The Mutakallimun's Proof for the Indivisible Atom
Presents the rational proof for the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments used by the mutakallimun to prove that division must end at an indivisible atom.

Core Idea
A finite object cannot contain infinite divisions.

Having defined the positions, Al-Juwayni now presents the mutakallimun's proof. It consists of two linked arguments: one based on the finitude of boundaries, the other on the necessary relationship between connection and separation. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'ودليل المتكلم' to the end. Notice the two arguments introduced by 'أن' and 'وأيضاً'.
2
Analyze the first argument
The first argument states that a finite thing (limited in sides) cannot contain infinite divisions. Understand why that is impossible.
This is a direct rational intuition.
3
Analyze the second argument
The second argument uses connection (ittisal) and separation (infisal). It argues that because connection is sensed as finite, separation must be finite too, otherwise connection would be infinite.
This argument relies on the necessary precedence of connection for separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في إثبات الجوهر الفرد. الجسم ينتهي بالتجزئة إلى حد لا يقبل الوصف بالتجزي ويسميه المتكلمون جوهرًا فردًا، وصارت الفلاسفة إلى أنه لا ينتهي إلى حد لا يقبل الوصف بالتجزي. ومدار المسألة على أن الجسم عند المتكلم هو المركب من أجزاء متناهية، وما تحصره النهايات والأطراف لا يشتمل على ما لا نهاية له، وعند الفيلسوف الأجزاء إنما تحدث بالفعل في الجسم إما رضًا وكسرًا وإما بانتشاره وإما باختلاف عرضين وإما بالوهم والقوة، والجسم مركب من هيولى وصورة لا من أجزاء متحيزة. ودليل المتكلم في المسألة أن المتناهي أطرافه وأضلاعه يستحيل أن يشتمل على منقسمات بلا نهاية فإن المحصور بالنهايات لا يكون حاصرًا بما لا نهايات له، وأيضًا فإن الاتصال المحسوس في الجسم متناه بالحس والانفصال يستدعي سبق اتصال لا محالة، فلو كان الجسم مما ينفصل عن النهاية فليكن فيه الاتصال لغير النهاية، فإن كان الانفصال بالفعل فيسبقه الاتصال بالفعل وإن كان الانفصال بالقوة والوهم فليكن فيه الاتصال بالقوة والوهم، ثم اتصاله متناء قوة وفعلا.
Issue on proving the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). The body ends by division at a limit that does not accept description by division, and the mutakallimun call it an indivisible atom. The philosophers have taken the position that it does not end at a limit that does not accept description by division. If the separation is actual, then actual connection precedes it; if the separation is potential and hypothetical, then let there be in it potential and hypothetical connection. Then its connection is finite in potentiality and actuality.

Commentary
This passage presents the classical kalam proof for the existence of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard). It begins by defining the issue: the body, when divided, reaches a limit that cannot be further divided, called by the mutakallimun an indivisible atom. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies on physical experiments or sense data alone.
Correction: The proof is rational and a priori, using logical necessity and the nature of finitude, not empirical measurement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Have you ever truly reflected on the fact that everything you see, touch, and experience is built from finite, limited components? How does this realization challenge the way you view your own dependence on Allah?
?
The proof hinges on the idea that a finite being cannot contain infinite divisions. Your own life is finite—limited in days, strength, and knowledge. When you consider that your existence, like the atom, has an end, does it move you to prioritize what is eternal over what is temporary? What one change can you make today to align your life with this truth?
?
If the mutakallimun could use reason to affirm a deeper reality about the physical world, what does that demand from you regarding the use of your intellect in matters of faith? Are you actively seeking to understand the evidences for your beliefs, or are you relying on blind imitation? What step will you take this week to strengthen your conviction through knowledge?

The Indivisible Atom: Defining the Debate
The author defines the indivisible atom and contrasts the mutakallim and philosopher views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core disagreement between the mutakallimun and the philosophers regarding the composition of physical bodies and the definition of the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun and philosophers fundamentally disagree on whether bodies are composed of finite or infinite parts.

This lesson introduces the central issue of the indivisible atom. The author states the mutakallim's position that division of a body eventually reaches an indivisible part, and contrasts it with the philosophers' view that division never ends. Understanding this foundational difference is crucial before examining the proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two positions.
Pay attention to the terminology used.
2
Identify the definitions
Note the definition of body according to the mutakallim and the philosopher. The mutakallim says body is composed of finite parts; the philosopher says it is composed of hyle and form.
3
Reflect on the disagreement
Consider why this disagreement matters for theology and natural philosophy.
The mutakallim's view supports creation and divine intervention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في إثبات الجوهر الفرد. الجسم ينتهي بالتجزئة إلى حد لا يقبل الوصف بالتجزي ويسميه المتكلمون جوهرا فردا، وصارت الفلاسفة إلى أنه لا ينتهي إلى حد لا يقبل الوصف بالتجزي. ومدار المسألة على أن الجسم عند المتكلم هو المركب من أجزاء متناهية، وما تحصره النهايات والأطراف لا يشتمل على ما لا نهاية له، وعند الفيلسوف الأجزاء إنما تحدث بالفعل في الجسم إما رضا وكسرا، وإما بانتشاره، وإما باختلاف عرضين وإما بالوهم والقوة، والجسم مركب من هيولى وصورة لا من أجزاء متحيزة. ودليل المتكلم في المسألة أن المتناهي أطرافه وأضلاعه يستحيل أن يشتمل على منقسمات بلا نهاية فإن المحصور بالنهايات لا يكون حاصرا بما لا نهايات له، وأيضا فإن الاتصال المحسوس في الجسم متناه بالحس والانفصال يستدعي سبق اتصال لا محالة، فلو كان الجسم مما ينفصل عن النهاية، فليكن فيه الاتصال لغير النهاية، فإن كان الانفصال بالفعل فيسبقه الاتصال بالفعل وإن كان الانفصال بالقوة والوهم فليكن فيه الاتصال بالقوة والوهم، ثم اتصاله متناه قوة وفعلا، فانفصاله يجب أن يكون كذلك، وأيضا فإن المقدار الذي اشتمل عليه الجسم متناه فلو نصف الجسم نصفين، وكان أحد المقدارين يقبل التجزي إلى غير النهاية حتى يصير ذا مقادير بغير نهاية فيلزم أن يكون الأقل وهو مثل الأكثر وهو الجملة ويلزم أن يكون فيما لا يتناهى من المقادير تفاوت الأقل والأكثر، وكلاهما محال.
A question concerning the proof of the indivisible atom. The body ends by division to a limit that does not accept the description of division, and the mutakallimun call it an indivisible atom; the philosophers hold that it does not end at a limit that does not accept the description of division. The pivot of the question is that the body according to the mutakallim is composed of finite parts, and what is encompassed by ends and boundaries cannot contain that which has no limit. According to the philosopher, parts occur actually in the body either by breaking and splitting, or by its dispersal, or by difference of two accidents, or by estimation and potentiality; and the body is composed of hyle and form, not of spatially distinct parts. The proof of the mutakallim in this question is that that which has finite extremities and sides is impossible to contain infinite divisions, for what is limited by ends cannot encompass what has no ends. Also, the sensible continuity in the body is finite by sense, and discontinuity necessarily requires a preceding continuity. So if the body were such that it separates without end, then let there be in it continuity without end. If the separation is actual, then continuity precedes it actually; if the separation is by potentiality and estimation, then let there be in it continuity by potentiality and estimation. Then its continuity is finite in potentiality and actuality, so its separation must be likewise. Also, the quantity which the body contains is finite. So if we halve the body into two halves, and one of the two quantities accepts division to infinity such that it becomes infinite in quantities, then it would follow that the lesser which is like the greater which is the whole, and it would follow that among infinite quantities there is difference of lesser and greater, both of which are impossible.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage defines the issue of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) and presents the mutakallim's proof against infinite divisibility of body. It begins by stating the disagreement: the mutakallimun hold that body divides finitely to an indivisible atom, while philosophers deny this. The basis is the definition of body: for the mutakallim, body is composed of finite parts; for the philosopher, parts arise actually through breaking, dispersal, etc., and body is composed of hyle and form. Then three proofs are given: (1) A finite thing cannot contain infinite parts because what is bounded cannot encompass what is unbounded. (2) Sensible continuity is finite; discontinuity requires prior continuity; if body could be divided infinitely, then both continuity and discontinuity would be infinite, contradicting the finitude of sensed continuity. (3) If a finite quantity is halved repeatedly infinitely, the half would equal the whole, and there would be differences among infinite quantities, both absurd.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
الجسم
التجزئة
المتناهي
الهيولى
الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate is about whether atoms exist in general rather than the specific composition of body.
Correction: The debate specifically concerns whether body is composed of finite parts that end in an indivisible atom or is infinitely divisible.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallim, what is body composed of?
A) Hyle and form
B) Finite parts ending in indivisible atoms
C) Infinite parts
D) A single continuous substance

Key Takeaway
The core disagreement is whether body is composed of finite parts (mutakallim) or hyle and form (philosophers), leading to different views on the existence of an indivisible atom.

Proof 1: The Bounded Cannot Contain the Unbounded
The author presents the first proof of the mutakallim against infinite divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational argument used by the mutakallimun to prove that a finite body cannot contain an infinite number of parts.

Core Idea
A finite body, limited by its boundaries, cannot encompass an infinite number of divisions.

This lesson focuses on the first proof presented by the mutakallim. The argument is simple yet powerful: if a body has finite dimensions, then it cannot contain an infinite number of parts, because what is bounded by endpoints cannot embrace that which is without end.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the Arabic and translation of the first proof.
Notice the concise language.
2
Understand the logic
The proof states that a thing with finite extremities cannot contain infinite divisions. Reflect on why this is logically necessary.
3
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: Could a bounded container hold an unbounded number of items? This is the core intuition.
This principle is a cornerstone of kalam arguments about finitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في إثبات الجوهر الفرد. الجسم ينتهي بالتجزئة إلى حد لا يقبل الوصف بالتجزي ويسميه المتكلمون جوهرا فردا، وصارت الفلاسفة إلى أنه لا ينتهي إلى حد لا يقبل الوصف بالتجزي. ومدار المسألة على أن الجسم عند المتكلم هو المركب من أجزاء متناهية، وما تحصره النهايات والأطراف لا يشتمل على ما لا نهاية له، وعند الفيلسوف الأجزاء إنما تحدث بالفعل في الجسم إما رضا وكسرا، وإما بانتشاره، وإما باختلاف عرضين وإما بالوهم والقوة، والجسم مركب من هيولى وصورة لا من أجزاء متحيزة. ودليل المتكلم في المسألة أن المتناهي أطرافه وأضلاعه يستحيل أن يشتمل على منقسمات بلا نهاية فإن المحصور بالنهايات لا يكون حاصرا بما لا نهايات له، وأيضا فإن الاتصال المحسوس في الجسم متناه بالحس والانفصال يستدعي سبق اتصال لا محالة، فلو كان الجسم مما ينفصل عن النهاية، فليكن فيه الاتصال لغير النهاية، فإن كان الانفصال بالفعل فيسبقه الاتصال بالفعل وإن كان الانفصال بالقوة والوهم فليكن فيه الاتصال بالقوة والوهم، ثم اتصاله متناه قوة وفعلا، فانفصاله يجب أن يكون كذلك، وأيضا فإن المقدار الذي اشتمل عليه الجسم متناه فلو نصف الجسم نصفين، وكان أحد المقدارين يقبل التجزي إلى غير النهاية حتى يصير ذا مقادير بغير نهاية فيلزم أن يكون الأقل وهو مثل الأكثر وهو الجملة ويلزم أن يكون فيما لا يتناهى من المقادير تفاوت الأقل والأكثر، وكلاهما محال.
A question concerning the proof of the indivisible atom. Then its continuity is finite in potentiality and actuality, so its separation must be likewise. Also, the quantity which the body contains is finite. According to the first proof, what is impossible for a body with finite extremities?
A) To contain any division
B) To contain infinite divisions

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body is finite, bounded, and limited. Yet you chase infinite desires, infinite distractions, infinite worries. This proof shows the impossibility of the bounded containing the unbounded. What finite thing in your life are you trying to fill with infinite meaning? How will you redirect that longing to the only Infinite today?
?
The mutakallim used this proof to humble the intellect, showing that even physical realities have logical limits. When was the last time you admitted your own limitations before Allah? What is one area of your life where you need to stop pretending you are unbounded and instead submit to the Only One who is Infinite?
?
If a finite body cannot hold infinite divisions, how can your finite mind claim to fully comprehend the Creator? What does this lesson teach you about the proper attitude when learning about Allah's attributes? Will you approach with humility or arrogance?

Proofs 2 and 3: Continuity and the Halving Paradox
The author presents the second and third proofs of the mutakallim against infinite divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional arguments: one based on the nature of continuity and discontinuity, and another based on the absurdity of a half equalling the whole through infinite division.

Core Idea
Infinite division leads to contradictions in both continuity and quantity.

The author presents two further proofs, both showing that infinite division leads to absurd consequences. The first concerns the relationship between continuity and discontinuity; the second concerns the mathematical absurdity of a part equalling the whole.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof about continuity
Read the Arabic and translation of the second proof (from 'Also, the sensible continuity...' to 'so its separation must be likewise').
This proof uses the observed finitude of continuity.
2
Understand the continuity-discontinuity logic
If division could be infinite, then both continuity and discontinuity would be infinite, but sensed continuity is finite. Therefore infinite division is impossible.
3
Read the third proof about halving
Read the halving argument (from 'Also, the quantity...' to the end).
4
Grasp the halving paradox
If a finite length is halved infinitely, the halves become infinite in number, leading to the absurdity that a part equals the whole.
This resembles Zeno's paradox but is used here to reject actual infinite division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في إثبات الجوهر الفرد. الجسم ينتهي بالتجزئة إلى حد لا يقبل الوصف بالتجزي ويسميه المتكلمون جوهرا فردا، وصارت الفلاسفة إلى أنه لا ينتهي إلى حد لا يقبل الوصف بالتجزي. ومدار المسألة على أن الجسم عند المتكلم هو المركب من أجزاء متناهية، وما تحصره النهايات والأطراف لا يشتمل على ما لا نهاية له، وعند الفيلسوف الأجزاء إنما تحدث بالفعل في الجسم إما رضا وكسرا، وإما بانتشاره، وإما باختلاف عرضين وإما بالوهم والقوة، والجسم مركب من هيولى وصورة لا من أجزاء متحيزة. ودليل المتكلم في المسألة أن المتناهي أطرافه وأضلاعه يستحيل أن يشتمل على منقسمات بلا نهاية فإن المحصور بالنهايات لا يكون حاصرا بما لا نهايات له، وأيضا فإن الاتصال المحسوس في الجسم متناه بالحس والانفصال يستدعي سبق اتصال لا محالة، فلو كان الجسم مما ينفصل عن النهاية، فليكن فيه الاتصال لغير النهاية، فإن كان الانفصال بالفعل فيسبقه الاتصال بالفعل وإن كان الانفصال بالقوة والوهم فليكن فيه الاتصال بالقوة والوهم، ثم اتصاله متناه قوة وفعلا، فانفصاله يجب أن يكون كذلك، وأيضا فإن المقدار الذي اشتمل عليه الجسم متناه فلو نصف الجسم نصفين، وكان أحد المقدارين يقبل التجزي إلى غير النهاية حتى يصير ذا مقادير بغير نهاية فيلزم أن يكون الأقل وهو مثل الأكثر وهو الجملة ويلزم أن يكون فيما لا يتناهى من المقادير تفاوت الأقل والأكثر، وكلاهما محال.

If the separation is actual, then continuity precedes it actually; if the separation is by potentiality and estimation, then let there be in it continuity by potentiality and estimation. Then its continuity is finite in potentiality and actuality, so its separation must be likewise. Also, the quantity which the body contains is finite. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The halving argument is about potential division, not actual infinite division.
Correction: The argument assumes actual infinite division (the quantity 'accepts division to infinity' actually), leading to contradictions in actual quantities.

Checkpoint
In the halving argument, what follows if a finite quantity is divided infinitely?
A) The half remains smaller than the whole
B) The half equals the whole
C) The division stops at the atom
D) The quantity becomes zero

Key Takeaway
These proofs show that infinite division leads to logical contradictions, supporting the mutakallim's conclusion that division must end in an indivisible atom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallim proves that infinite division is impossible. In your own life, where do you assume something will 'go on forever' (health, youth, opportunities) that actually has a finite limit set by Allah? Are you living with that reality or denying it?
?
This lesson shows that even in the smallest physical quantity there is a limit decreed by Allah. How does this affect the way you view the precision of Allah's creation? Do you treat the atoms around you—your time, your actions—as if they are infinite and cheap, or as finite and precious?

Refutation of the Objection of Potential Infinity
The author answers the objection that a body contains potentially infinite parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that a body contains potentially infinite parts does not refute the proof against actual infinity, and how the argument forces the objector into a contradiction.

Core Idea
Potential infinity is no escape; it either becomes actual or remains impossible.

The previous proof established that a finite body cannot contain infinitely many actual parts. Now an objector suggests that perhaps the body contains potentially infinite parts, and that this escapes the proof. In this passage, the author dismantles this objection by examining whether that potentiality can ever be actualized, leading to a decisive refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector concedes that actual infinite parts are impossible but claims potential infinite parts are possible. Note the key distinction between actual (bil-fi'l) and potential (bil-quwwa).
2
Analyze the refutation
The argument forces the objector to choose: either the potential can become actual or not. If not, it's an impossible potential. If yes, actualizing it leads to the same absurdities.
This is a classic dilemma argument.
3
Consider the analogy
The author gives an example of adding bodies infinitely to show that imagination does not imply real possibility. This reinforces the role of rational proof over pure estimation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: ما ذكرتموه صحيح في تقدير أجزاء الجسم بالفعل، فإن الجسم المتناهي لا يشتمل على أجزاء بالفعل غير متناهية لكنا نقول: هو يشتمل على أجزاء بالقوة غير متناهية، فالمحصور في الفعل لا يحصر غير المتناهي بالفعل، فلم لا يجوز أن يحصر غير المتناهي بالقوة وفيه النزاع؟ قلنا: ما قدرتموه بالقوة أيجوز في العقل أن يخرج إلى الفعل، فإن لم يجز ظهرت الاستحالة وبقي الوهم المجرد الذي دل على خلافه برهان العقل أوإن جاز خروجه إلى الفعل، وقدرنا خروجه إلى الفعل لزم المحال الذي ذكرنا هذا، كما يقال ما من جسم إلا ويمكن أن يتصل به جسم آخر، بحيث لا يقف الوهم إلى حد ونهاية، ثم لا يدل ذلك على أن جسما لا يتناهى متصور الوجود، بل الاتصال في الجسم يجوز إلى حد لا إلى هذا المحال، وهو تصور جسم لا يتناهى وإن بقي الوهم في حال التقدير الوهمي ينتهي.
For it is said: What you have mentioned is correct regarding the estimation of the parts of the body actually; for a finite body does not contain infinite parts actually, but we say: it contains parts potentially infinite. So the finite actually does not contain the infinite actually, so why may it not contain the infinite potentially, and this is the point of dispute? We say: What you have estimated by potentiality, is it permissible in reason to come into actuality? If it is not permissible, then impossibility becomes evident, and the mere estimation remains, which the rational proof has shown to be contrary. Or if its coming into actuality is permissible, and we estimate its coming into actuality, then the impossibility which we mentioned follows. This is like saying: There is no body except that it is possible for another body to be connected to it such that estimation does not stop at a limit and an end. Then this does not indicate that a body without end is conceivable in existence; rather, connection in body is permissible to a limit, not to this impossibility, which is the conception of a body without end, and even if the estimation remains in the state of estimative estimation, it ends.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous proof and its refutation. The objector argues that the proof only applies to actual parts, but the body contains potentially infinite parts, so the proof does not rule out potential infinity. The answer: potential infinity either can become actual or not. If it cannot become actual, then the potential is impossible and the mere imagination is contradicted by rational proof. If it can become actual, then the same absurdities (half equals whole, etc.) would occur when actualized. An analogy: one can imagine adding bodies infinitely, but that does not prove an actually infinite body is possible; connection in body is limited to a finite limit, not to an infinite impossibility.

Key Terms
بالفعل
بالقوة
غير المتناهي
الوهم
البرهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Potential infinity is a coherent escape from the proof against actual infinity.
Correction: Potential infinity either cannot be actualized (making it impossible) or can be actualized (leading to the same absurdities).

Checkpoint
According to the objector, what kind of infinite parts does a finite body contain?
A) Actual infinite parts
B) Potential infinite parts
C) Both actual and potential
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The argument refutes potential infinity by showing it either remains impossible or leads to the same contradictions as actual infinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine something going on forever—like a chain of causes or an endless series of days—your mind can continue without stopping. But this lesson shows that what is merely imaginable is not necessarily possible in reality. Does your trust in your own reasoning ever lead you to believe something is possible just because you can picture it? How will you guard against confusing imagination with reality in your understanding of Allah's creation?
?
The author forces a dilemma: either potential infinity cannot become actual, which makes it a mere illusion, or it can become actual, which leads to contradiction. In your own life, do you rely on 'potential' excuses or possibilities that you never intend to actualize, using them to avoid facing a difficult truth? What is one area where you need to stop hiding behind 'potential' and confront what is actually required of you?
?
If the universe cannot contain an actual infinite, and even potential infinity fails to explain its existence, then the universe must have a beginning. Does this truth settle in your heart, moving you to gratitude and submission to the Creator who brought it into existence from nothing? Or do you still find yourself resisting, holding onto imaginary escapes? Commit today to one action that reflects your conviction that Allah alone is the Eternal, the First.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Condition for Imagination's Truth
The author states the condition for imagination's truth and argues that infinite divisibility violates it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why imagination must be checked by reason when it leads to the notion of infinite parts, and how the author argues that such an imagination is actually untruthful.

Core Idea
Imagination is truthful only when it does not lead to actual infinites.

In this passage, the author tackles the reliability of imagination in reasoning about infinite divisibility. He establishes a crucial condition: imagination only yields truth if it does not lead to infinite parts. This contradicts reason, and the rest of the passage unpacks the implications, from potentiality to the infinite regress of imagination itself, culminating in a critique of philosophers who were inconsistent in their application of this principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the central condition
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the opening statement: 'الوهم إنما يصدق بشرط أن لا يؤدي إلى أجزاء غير متناهية'. Ask yourself why imagination might be unreliable when it leads to infinite parts.
2
Understand the opponent's position
The author mentions that opponents concede that actual infinite parts are inconceivable but disagree about potentiality and imagination. Notice how the author addresses each of these separately in the following lines.
This step helps you follow the argument structure.
3
Examine the argument from potentiality
The author argues that matter (hyle) in potentiality cannot accept infinite divisions or unions. Reflect on why this is relevant: it shows that potentiality also has limits.
This reinforces the claim that infinite processes are not possible even in potentiality.
4
Examine the argument from imagination
The author shows that imagination does not stop at any limit: it can always imagine further increases (like adding a universe) or decreases (dividing a body). This leads to an infinite regress, contradicting reason.
This demonstrates why imagination alone cannot be trusted.
5
Analyze the critique of philosophers
Some philosophers correctly reject void beyond the world because bodies are finite, but they wrongly affirm infinite parts inside the world. Note the inconsistency: they apply finitude externally but not internally.
This shows the challenge of consistently applying the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول هاهنا : أن الوهم إنما يصدق بشرط أن لا يؤدي إلى أجزاء غير متناهية وذلك خلاف العقل، وهذا آخر ما ينتهي إليه نظر الناظر في هذه المسألة، فإن الخصم ربما يساعد على أن الأجزاء التي هي غير متناهية بالفعل في الجسم غير متصور، وإنما يخالف في القوة والوهم، وقد ثبت في القوة أن هيولي الجسم بقوته لا تقوى على قبول انفصالات بلا نهاية، كما لا تقوى على قبول اتصالات بلا نهاية، وقد بينا في الوهم أن الوهم لا يقف إلى حد لا يتوهم زيادة على الجسم المحدود كزيادة عالم آخر، وزيادة فضاء وخلاء وراء العالم، كذلك لا يقف إلى حد لا يتوهم نقصانا على الجسم إلى نقصان آخر حتى يقدر ملاقي الجسم لا يتناهى، وبعض الفلاسفة يفطن فيما وراء العالم فلم يثبت خلاء وفضاء بتناهي الأجسام، وما يفطن فيما هو داخل العالم، فأثبت املا وفيضا بلا تناهي الأجزاء، ولا خارج من المحيط، ولا داخل في المركز، والله أعلم.
And we say here: Indeed imagination is only truthful on condition that it does not lead to infinite parts, and that is contrary to reason. This is the final point to which the reflection of the one who reflects on this issue reaches. Indeed, the opponent might concede that the parts which are actually infinite in the body are inconceivable, but he only disagrees regarding potentiality and imagination. And it has been established regarding potentiality that the matter of the body by its potentiality is not capable of receiving divisions without end, just as it is not capable of receiving unions without end. And we have shown concerning imagination that imagination does not stop at a limit beyond which it cannot imagine an increase upon the limited body, like the increase of another universe, and the increase of space and void beyond the universe; likewise, it does not stop at a limit beyond which it cannot imagine a decrease upon the body to another decrease, so that it estimates the body's contact (with something) without end. And some of the philosophers are insightful concerning what is beyond the universe, so they did not affirm void and space due to the finitude of bodies; and they are not insightful concerning what is inside the universe, so they affirmed extension and space with infinite parts — neither outside the circumference nor inside the centre. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about the limits of imagination (al-wahm) in relation to infinite divisibility. The author begins by stating that imagination is only truthful when it does not lead to infinite parts, which is contrary to reason ('ذلك خلاف العقل'). He then explains that the opponent might concede that actual infinite parts in a body are inconceivable, but disagrees regarding potentiality and imagination. Next, the author argues that the matter of the body in potentiality cannot receive infinite divisions or unions. He then shows that imagination does not stop at a limit: it can always imagine further increases (like another universe or void beyond the world) or further decreases (division of a body into smaller parts endlessly). Some philosophers correctly recognize no void beyond the universe because bodies are finite, but they mistakenly posit infinite extension inside the universe — neither outside the circumference nor inside the centre. The author asserts that this is a failure of insight, concluding with 'والله أعلم'.

Key Terms
الوهم (al-wahm)
أجزاء غير متناهية (infinite parts)
القوة (potentiality)
هيولي (hyle/matter)
الفلاسفة (philosophers)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that imagination alone can verify the possibility of infinite divisibility.
Correction: The author shows that when imagination leads to infinite parts, it is actually untruthful and must be checked by reason.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the condition for imagination (al-wahm) to be truthful?
A) That it agrees with the senses
B) That it does not lead to infinite parts
C) That it is consistent with the philosophers
D) That it imagines only finite bodies

Key Takeaway
Imagination is truthful only when it does not lead to infinite parts; otherwise, it contradicts reason and must be rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about your faith, do you ever follow your imagination without checking whether it leads to an infinite regress? What would it take for you to stop and say, 'This violates reason'?
?
The author points out that some philosophers were insightful about what is outside the universe but blind inside it. Where might you be intellectually consistent in theory but fail to apply that consistency to your own life or heart?
?
Imagination is only truthful when it doesn't lead to absurdity. Is there a belief you hold that, if pressed to its logical conclusion, would lead to something impossible? Will you examine it honestly today?
